General Rules of Liability for Harm by Animals: Mursil, Sa'iq, Raakib, Naakhis
Collection of rulings with rational proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for liability of the driver (sa'iq), releaser (mursil), and provoker (naakhis), and distinguish the conditions that impose or remove liability.

Core Idea
An animal's handler is liable only when the harm flows directly from his action; severed causation shifts liability.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents the Hanafi position on liability for harm caused by animals, where causation is the decisive factor. You will learn when the driver, releaser, or provoker must pay compensation and when no one is liable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four roles
Name the four parties: sa'iq (driver), raakib (rider), naakhis (provoker), mursil (releaser). Understand their actions.
The passage focuses on mursil and naakhis more than raakib.
2
Determine the condition for liability
For each role, note the condition that triggers liability: immediate control or causation. For mursil, liability exists only if harm follows immediately without pause.
Imam Abu Yusuf's view on immediate vs delayed is treated in a separate lesson.
3
Apply to specific examples
Work through the cases: bird with no driver, dog on road, crop damage, and the provoker's instigation. State who is liable and why.
The passage gives rulings for each scenario.
4
Note the exceptions
Identify cases where liability is removed: if the animal acts after a pause, the release is severed. Also, a hunting dog that immediately attacks a person in the field does not make the releaser liable.
This exception is crucial for understanding causation.

Source Text

قائد کی مکار برادی رابطہ کے [؟] عاقلہ پر رجوع کریں۔ کسی نے اپنا چوپایہ قطار میں باندھ دیا، قائد کو اس کا علم نہ ہوا تو ضامن رابط ہو گا۔ کسی نے چوپایہ چھوڑا اور اس کا ہانکنے والا تھا، چوپائے نے کسی پر حملہ کر کے اسے ہلاک کر دیا، سائق پر ضمان ہے۔ پرندہ (باز) چھوڑا اور اس کو ہانکا، اس پرندے نے کسی مملوک شکار کو قتل کر دیا، ضامن نہ ہوگا۔ کتا چھوڑا اور اس کا کوئی سائق نہیں تھا، کوئی ضامن نہیں ہوگا۔ امام ابو یوسف کا نقطہ نظر: کسی نے چوپایہ چھوڑا، اس نے فورا کسی پر حملہ کر دیا تو مرسل ضامن ہے۔ ارسال کے بعد چوپایہ نے وقفہ کیا پھر شکار پر دوڑا تو ارسال منقطع سمجھا جائے گا یا نہیں؟ کتے کو شکار پر چھوڑا، اس نے فورا کسی نفس یا مال پر حملہ کر دیا تو مرسل ضامن نہ ہوگا اور راستہ میں چھوڑنے کی وجہ سے ضامن ہوگا۔ چوپایہ چھوڑا، اس نے فورا کھیتی خراب کر دی تو مرسل ضامن ہے۔ قصاب کی بکری کی آنکھ پھوڑی، جتنی قیمت میں کمی ہوئی اتنی مقدار واجب ہوگی۔ قصائی کی گائے، اونٹ، گدھے، خچر، گھوڑے کی آنکھ پھوڑ دی، اسکا کیا حکم ہے؟ اونٹ گائے وغیرہ کی آنکھ میں چوتھائی قیمت کیوں واجب ہوتی ہے، اس کی عقلی دلیل۔ ایک شخص اپنے چوپائے پر بیٹھا جارہا تھا کہ دوسرے نے آر مار دی جس سے وہ بدکا اور کسی کو ہلاک کر دیا تو ضمان آر مار کرنے والے پر ہے۔ مضمان مذکور ناخس پر ہے راکب پر نہیں، اسکی عقلی دلیل۔ ناس کو چوپائے نے لات مار کر ہلاک کر دیا تو اس کا خون بدر ہے اور اگر سوار بھی ہلاک ہو گیا تو دیت ناخس کی عاقلہ پر ہے۔ ناخس کے چھیڑنے سے چوپایہ نے پر کودا اور اسے ہلاک کر دیا تو ضمان ناخس پر ہوگا۔ امام ابو یوسف کا نقطہ نظر: ناخس نے راکب کی اجازت سے آر مار دی، چوپایہ بدکا اور کسی کو روند ڈالا تو دیت دونوں پر ہے۔ سوال مقدر کا جواب، مذکورہ مسئلہ کی نظیر، ناخس راکب سے ضمان لے گا یا نہیں؟ مسئلہ مذکورہ کے شواہد۔ بچہ کے ہاتھ میں ہتھیار تھا جس سے کوئی مرگیا تو بچہ پر ضمان ہے، ناخس پر کب ضمان ہے اور کب نہیں؟ اگر روند ناخس کے فورا بعد پایا جائے تو ضمان کس پر ہوگا؟ قائد یا سائق ہو اور ناخس نے چوپایہ کو چھوڑ دیا جس سے علی الفور کوئی مر گیا تو ضمان ناخس پر ہے۔ راستہ میں کسی نے ایسی چیز کھڑی کر دی جو چوپایہ کو چھوئی اور وہ بدک گیا اور کسی کو ہلاک کر دیا تو ضمان چیز کھڑی کرنے والے پر ہوگا۔

Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings (masa'il) on liability for harm caused by animals, integrated with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, particularly the view of Imam Abu Yusuf. The passage type is a collection of rulings with rational proofs (aqli dalil) and occasional disputes. The author follows the order of the Hidayah, expanding on each scenario. First, it discusses liability of a driver (sa'iq) who is with the animal: if he releases and the animal kills, he is liable. For a bird or dog without a driver, no liability. Then Imam Abu Yusuf's view: immediate attack after release makes the releaser (mursil) liable; if the animal pauses and then attacks, the release is considered severed and the releaser may not be liable. For a dog released for hunting that immediately attacks a person, the releaser is not liable for that attack, but if he released it on a road, he is liable for any damage. Damage to crops: immediate damage makes releaser liable. Then a specific ruling on poking the eye of a butcher's goat: compensation is the decrease in value. For larger animals (camel, cow, etc.), one-fourth of the value is obligatory, and the text promises a rational proof (aqli dalil). Next, a rider on an animal: if another person kicks the animal causing it to shy and kill, the kicker (not the rider) is liable. If the animal kicks and kills someone, the victim's blood is wasted (no compensation) if the victim was the provoker, but if the rider also dies, blood-money is on the provoker's 'aqilah. If the provoker's instigation causes the animal to jump and kill the provoker himself, the liability is on the provoker. Imam Abu Yusuf holds: if the provoker acts with the rider's permission, both are liable. The text then poses and answers assumed questions: whether the provoker can recover from the rider, and analogies. Finally, rulings on a child with a weapon, and on someone placing an object that startles the animal. Throughout, the Hanafi position and its internal differences are presented with rational justifications.

Key Terms
سائق (sa'iq): The person who drives or herds the animal, controlling its movement.
راکب (raakib): The rider of the animal.
ناخس (naakhis): The person who provokes or instigates the animal to act.
مرسل (mursil): The person who releases or lets loose an animal.
ضامن (damin): Liable for compensation or blood-money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a person releases a dog in a road, and the dog immediately attacks and kills someone, who is liable?
A) The person who released the dog (mursil)
B) The owner of the dog
C) No one, because dogs are naturally dangerous
D) The victim for being in the road

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, liability for harm by animals depends on direct causation and immediacy; exceptions exist for hunting animals and road releases.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Imam Abu Yusuf on the Releaser's Liability
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on mursil liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the releaser's liability for immediate vs. delayed animal attacks, and evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).

Core Idea
Imam Abu Yusuf holds the releaser liable for immediate attacks; Abu Hanifa may not, making causation timing the key difference.

This lesson focuses on the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf presented in the passage regarding the mursil (releaser). Imam Abu Yusuf argues that if the animal attacks immediately after release, the releaser is liable, but if there is a pause, the release is severed. The implied view of Abu Hanifa (from the general ruling) is that the releaser is not liable for immediate attacks in certain contexts. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply causation principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The dispute is about the mursil who releases an animal that subsequently causes harm: is he liable immediately after release, or only if the attack is immediate?
The passage quotes Imam Abu Yusuf's view explicitly.
2
State Imam Abu Yusuf's position
Imam Abu Yusuf says: if the animal attacks immediately after release, the mursil is liable. If the animal pauses and then attacks, the release is severed and the mursil may not be liable.
This is a stricter causal link.
3
Infer Abu Hanifa's position
From the earlier ruling that 'released a dog for hunting, it immediately attacks a person, the releaser is not liable', we infer Abu Hanifa holds that immediate attack does not always make the releaser liable, especially for hunting animals. The difference may hinge on the type of animal or the intention of release.
The passage does not directly state Abu Hanifa's view, but it is implicit in the contrasting ruling.
4
Analyze the rational basis
The dispute is rooted in whether the release itself is the cause (sabab) of the harm, or whether the animal's independent action severs the link. Abu Yusuf emphasizes the immediacy as evidence of a strong causal connection.
The passage mentions a rational proof ('aqli dalil) for related rulings.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In practice: if an animal attacks immediately, Abu Yusuf imposes liability on the releaser; Abu Hanifa may not (in hunting scenarios). This affects who pays compensation.
The mufta bihi (operative view) is not specified in the passage.

Source Text

Commentary

Key Terms
مرسل (mursil): The person who releases or lets loose an animal.
إرسال (irsal): The act of releasing an animal.
منقطع (munqati'): Severed, disconnected.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the practical consequence if a hunting dog immediately attacks a person after being released in an open field?
A) The releaser is liable
B) The releaser is not liable
C) The owner of the dog is liable
D) The victim is responsible

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on the releaser's liability hinges on whether immediate attack after release creates a direct causal link; the hunting dog and road exceptions show limiting principles.

Liability for a Slave's Unintentional Crime: The Master's Option vs. Direct Liability
Inter-madhhab dispute on master's liability for slave's crime

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the master's liability for a slave's unintentional crime and explain the practical difference with the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
The master may pay or surrender the slave; the victim cannot demand payment after choice.

A slave commits an unintentional crime. Who pays? The Hanafi school gives the master a choice: pay compensation (arsh/diyah) or hand over the slave. Imam al-Shafi'i holds the master directly liable. This lesson explores the dispute and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the two options the master has according to the Hanafis when a slave commits an unintentional crime.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position
Contrast the Shafi'i view that the master is directly liable for compensation.
3
Compare the proofs
Though the passage does not quote the proofs, recognize that each school relies on different Quranic verses and hadith interpretations.
4
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Explain how the Hanafi option limits the master's financial exposure compared to Shafi'i direct liability.
5
Note the mufta bihi
The Hanafi position (master's option) is the relied-upon view in the school.

باب جناية المملوك والجناية عليه. غلام کوئی جنایت خطاء کرے تو ضمان (جرمانہ) غلام پر ہے یا آقا پر؟ اقوال فقہاء: امام شافعی کی دلیل، احناف کی دلیل، امام شافعی کے استدلال کا جواب، حنفیہ کے مسلک کی وضاحت، آقا کو کیا چیز دینے کا اختیار ہے؟ جس چیز کو آقا نے اختیار کر لیا، ولی جنایت کو دوسری چیز اختیار کرنے کا حق نہیں۔ اعادہ جنایت کی صورت میں پہلے والا حکم جاری ہوگا۔ دو جنایتیں کرلیں تو آقا کا وہ غلام ولی جنایتین کے حوالے کرنے میں یا ارش (تاوان) ادا کرنے میں اختیار ہے۔ اگر بہت سے اشخاص پر جنایت کی تو وہ اپنے سب حصوں کے بقدر غلام کی قیمت سے وصول کریں گے۔ جب جنایات مختلف قسم کی ہوں تو آقا کو کیا اختیار ہے؟ اگر آقا نے غلام جانی کو آزاد کر دیا اور آقا کو جنایت کا علم نہ تھا تو آقا اس کی قیمت اور جنایت کے تاوان میں سے اقل کا ضامن ہے۔ ہ، متد بیر، استیلا د کا حکم بھی حق والا ہے (امام کرخی کا نقطہ نظر؟) مطلق جنایت میں قتل اور مادون النفس جنایت بھی شامل ہے۔ مجرم غلام کو فروخت کرنے سے مراد بیع بشرط الخيار للمشتری ہے۔ آقا غلام کو بیع فاسد کے ساتھ بیچ دے پھر کیا حکم ہے؟ تین مسائل کی وضاحت، چار مسائل کی وضاحت۔
Chapter of the crime of the slave and the crime upon him. If a slave commits an unintentional crime, is the compensation on the slave or on the master? Statements of the jurists: Imam Shafi'i's proof, the Hanafis' proof, the answer to Imam Shafi'i's argument, explanation of the Hanafi school, what is the master given the option to give? Whatever the master has chosen, the victim's guardian has no right to choose something else. In case of repetition of the crime, the previous ruling applies. If he commits two crimes, the master has the option to hand over that slave to the victims of the two crimes or to pay compensation. If he committed crimes against many persons, they will recover from the slave's price according to their shares. When the crimes are of different types, what option does the master have? If the master freed the slave without knowing of the crime, the master is liable for the lesser of the slave's value and the compensation for the crime. The ruling of a mudabbir and an umm walad is also upon this (Imam al-Karkhi's view?). Absolute crime includes both murder and injury below murder. By 'selling the criminal slave' is meant a sale with a condition of option for the buyer. If the master sells the slave with a void sale, then what is the ruling? Explanation of three issues, explanation of four issues.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter (bab) on crimes committed by a slave (mamluk) and crimes against a slave. The central question is: when a slave commits an unintentional crime (jinaayah khata'), who bears the liability—the slave or his master? The text presents the views of different jurists, specifically an inter-madhhab dispute between Imam al-Shafi'i and the Hanafis. Imam Shafi'i argued that the master is liable, while the Hanafis held that the master has an option. The Hanafi response to Shafi'i's proof is given, along with an explanation of the Hanafi school's position. According to the Hanafis, the master may either pay compensation (arsh/diyah) or hand over the slave to the victim to be sold for compensation. Once the master chooses one option, the victim cannot demand the other. If the slave repeats the crime, the same ruling applies. If the slave commits two separate crimes, the master still has the option to hand over the slave to both victims or pay compensation. If there are multiple victims, they share the slave's price proportionally. If the crimes are of different types (e.g., murder and injury), the master's options are discussed further. A special case: if the master frees the slave without knowledge of the crime, he is liable for the lesser of the slave's value or the compensation amount. The text then mentions that the same ruling applies to a mudabbir (slave promised freedom upon master's death) and an umm walad (slave mother of her master's child), based on the view of Imam al-Karkhi. The passage also clarifies that all types of violent crimes (both murder and injury) are included. Finally, the text discusses the meaning of 'selling the criminal slave'—it refers to a sale with a condition of option for the buyer (bay' bi-shart al-khiyar lil-mushtari). If the master sells the slave through a void sale (bay' fasid), further rulings apply. The passage ends by indicating explanations of three and four issues, but the details are not fully given in this excerpt.

Key Terms
مملوك (mamluk): A slave owned by a master.
جنایت خطاء (jinaayah khata'): An unintentional crime, such as accidental killing or injury.
ولى جنایت (wali al-jinaayah): The victim or the victim's guardian who has the right to demand compensation or retribution.
ارش (arsh): Compensation for injuries below the level of murder, calculated based on the severity.
مدبر (mudabbir): A slave who has been promised freedom upon the master's death.
استیلاد (istilaad): A slave woman who bears her master's child (umm walad).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) He may either pay compensation or hand over the slave
C) The slave is executed
D) The master is not liable at all

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school grants the master of a slave who commits an unintentional crime a binding choice between paying compensation and surrendering the slave.

Master's Liability for Crimes of Mudabbar and Umm Walad
Rulings on master's guarantee for crimes by mudabbar and umm walad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for master's liability when a mudabbar or umm walad commits a crime, apply it to successive crimes, and distinguish the effects of court-ordered versus voluntary payment.

Core Idea
Master pays the lesser of the slave's value and the injury's indemnity — liability is limited to one value per slave.

This passage from the Hanafi commentary clarifies the master's financial responsibility when his mudabbar or umm walad commits a crime. The master is guarantor, but his liability is capped: he pays only the lesser of the slave's market value or the injury's arsh. The ruling includes important details about successive crimes, the effect of payment method, and what happens if the master later frees the slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic rule
The master is guarantor for the lesser of the slave's market value (qīma) and the indemnity for the injury (arsh). This is the core principle.
2
Understand the rationale
The master's liability is limited because the slave is property; the master is not responsible beyond the slave's value. This prevents unlimited liability.
3
Apply to successive crimes
If the slave commits multiple crimes while still with the master, only one value is due in total. The value is a single guarantee attached to the slave.
4
Distinguish payment scenarios
If the master pays by court order, no further liability for later crimes. If he pays voluntarily, the victim's guardian can choose to pursue the master or the previous guardian.
5
Examine special cases
If the master frees the slave after crimes, each crime requires a separate value. The slave's confession does not bind the master.

مدبر اور ام ولد نے کوئی جنایت کی تو آقا اس کی قیمت اور جنایت کے ارش میں سے اقل کا ضامن ہوگا

امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل
ثانی ولی جنایت اولی کا کب شریک ہوتا ہے

مدبر نے جنایت خطاء کا اقرار کیا تو اقرار سے مولیٰ پر کچھ لازم نہیں ہوگا
Chapter on the injury of mudabbar and umm walad. If a mudabbar or umm walad commits a crime, the master is guarantor for the lesser of its value and the arsh of the crime. The lesser of value and arsh is obligatory. Successive crimes of a mudabbar make only one value obligatory. If the mudabbar commits a second crime while the master had already paid for the first by the judge's order, there is no liability on the master. If the master paid the value without a judge's order, the guardian of the crime has the option to pursue the master or the previous guardian. The proof of Imam Abu Hanifa. When does the second guardian share with the first? If the master frees the mudabbar and he commits many crimes, each value is due on the master. If the mudabbar confesses to a crime of error, nothing becomes necessary on the master due to the confession.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage from the commentary discusses the master's liability for injuries caused by a mudabbar or umm walad. The core ruling is that when such a slave commits a crime, the master must pay the lesser of the slave's market value or the injury's indemnity (arsh). This is because the master's liability is limited; he is not responsible beyond the slave's value. For successive crimes, only one value is due as long as the slave remains in his possession, because the value is a single obligation tied to the slave. If the master pays by court order, subsequent crimes do not incur additional liability. If he pays voluntarily, the victim's guardian can choose whom to pursue. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is based on the principle of limited liability. If the master frees the mudabbar after crimes, each crime requires a separate value since the slave becomes free and the master's liability resets. Confession of the mudabbar to a crime of error does not bind the master because the slave's admission cannot impose liability on the master.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who has been promised freedom by the master upon the master's death (tadbir).
ام ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master.
ارش (arsh): Indemnity or compensation for a physical injury, determined by the injury's severity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The master's guarantee for crimes of mudabbar and umm walad is limited to the lesser of the slave's value and the injury's arsh, with successive crimes only incurring one value.

Usurper Liability for Injuries and Deaths
Rulings on liability when a slave is usurped and injured or commits crimes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the liability of a usurper for injuries or death of a slave or free child, applying the Hanafi rules on causation and timing.

Core Idea
Usurper liable only if injury occurs during his possession and is not directly caused by the owner or natural death.

This lesson examines key scenarios from the chapter on usurpation (ghasb) where a slave or free child is injured or dies while in the usurper's possession. The Hanafi school distinguishes between injuries caused before or during usurpation, and between natural and external causes of death, leading to different liability outcomes. Understanding these rulings requires careful analysis of causation (talil) and the principle that the usurper is a guarantor only for harms occurring under his control.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of victim
Determine whether the victim is a regular slave, a coerced slave (majbur alayh), a mudabbar (slave promised freedom upon master's death), or a free child. Each category has specific rules.
Coerced slaves and mudabbar have limited capacity; free children lack legal responsibility.
2
Determine the cause of injury or death
Was the injury caused by the master's direct act (e.g., cutting the slave's hand), by the slave's own action (e.g., injuring another), or by an external event (snakebite, lightning)? Natural death (fever) is also considered.
The cause affects whether the usurper's possession is deemed contributory.
3
Establish timing relative to usurpation
Did the injury occur before the usurper took possession, during his possession, or after return to the master? The timing is critical: if the master cut before usurpation, the usurper is liable for the cut's value; if during, the usurper is not liable.
The master's direct act during usurpation overrides the usurper's responsibility.
4
Apply the Hanafi rule
For slaves: If the injury occurs during usurpation and is not the master's direct act, the usurper is liable for the value or the diyah. For free children: Only external causes (snakebite, lightning) trigger diyah on the usurper's aqila; natural death no liability.
Istihsan overrides qiyas for children lacking capacity.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi disputes
For mudabbar slaves committing multiple crimes in different possession periods, Imam Muhammad and the two shaykhs differed on apportionment of liability between master and usurper. Also, disputes exist on deposit liability for children and coerced slaves.
The mufta bihi positions are stated in the text.

باب غصب العبد و المُدبّر والصبي والجناية في ذالك

Chapter on usurpation of the slave, mudabbar, child, and injury therein. If one cuts his slave's hand, then someone usurps him and he dies due to the cut in the usurper's hand, the usurper must pay the value of the cut. If the master cuts the hand while it is in the usurper's possession and the slave dies due to the cut in the usurper's possession, there is no guarantee on the usurper. If a coerced slave injures another coerced slave, then he dies in the usurper's possession, the usurper is liable. If someone usurps a mudabbar and the mudabbar commits a crime while with him, then he returns the mudabbar to his master, and then he commits another crime with the master, then the master is liable for its value which is half and half between them. The master will claim half from the usurper. The master will give half of the value to the first guardian of the crime. Then will he claim from the usurper? Statements of the jurists. Proof of Imam Muhammad and proof of the two shaykhs. [If] he committed a crime with the master, then someone usurped him, then he committed a second crime with the usurper, the master is liable for its value, half and half between them, and the master will claim half from the usurper. Ruling: if the slave committed a crime with the usurper, the usurper returned him, then the slave committed a second crime with the master. If someone usurped a mudabbar, the mudabbar committed a crime with the usurper, then he was returned to the master, then he was usurped again, then a second crime with the usurper, the master is liable for its value which is half and half between them. The master will give half of the value to the first guardian of the crime. The master will claim from the usurper. The reason for Imam Muhammad's distinction between the two cases. If someone usurps a free child and the child dies in the usurper's possession from sudden death or fever, nothing is due on the usurper; but if he dies from snakebite or lightning, diyah is on the usurper's aqila. The Hanafi istihsan proof. A slave was deposited with a child and the child killed him, then diyah is on the child's aqila. Similarly, if food was deposited and the child ate it, is the child liable? Statements of the jurists. If property was deposited with a coerced slave and he destroyed it, is he liable? The disagreement is regarding the child, its application.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage from the commentary covers multiple scenarios under the chapter of usurpation (ghasb). It begins with cases where a master severs his slave's hand, then the slave is usurped and dies; the usurper's liability depends on who caused the death. If the master cut the hand before usurpation, the usurper is liable for the value of the cut because his usurpation contributed to the loss. But if the master cut while the slave was in usurper's possession, the usurper is not liable since the master's act is the direct cause. For coerced slaves (majbur alayh), if one injures another and the victim dies in usurper's possession, the usurper is liable because he is responsible for the safety of the slave under his control. The mudabbar cases involve complex apportionment: when a mudabbar commits crimes both with the usurper and after return to the master, the master's total liability is the slave's value, shared equally between the two crimes. The master can recover half from the usurper. Imam Muhammad distinguishes between different sequences of crimes and possession. For free children, the usurper is not liable for natural deaths but is liable for external causes like snakebite, with diyah on his aqila. The Hanafi position here uses istihsan (juristic preference) over strict qiyas, considering the child's lack of capacity. The final part discusses deposits with children and coerced slaves, where liability falls on the child's aqila or the coerced slave's master accordingly.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Unlawful usurpation or seizure of property from rightful owner.
مجبور علیہ (majbur alayh): A slave who is coerced or forced to act, but here probably meaning a slave who is under compulsion or a specific legal category.
عاقلہ (aqila): The clan or group responsible for paying diyah on behalf of the killer.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave promised freedom upon the master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The master is liable, not the usurper
C) Both are equally liable
D) Neither is liable

Key Takeaway
The usurper's liability hinges on whether the injury occurred before or during his possession and whether it was caused naturally or externally; direct acts of the owner absolve the usurper.

Liability of a Usurper for a Free Child's Death: Hanafi Ruling with Istihsan
Ruling with Hanafi istihsan proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a usurped free child dies from natural vs. external causes, and explain the istihsan analogy that supports this ruling.

Core Idea
Natural death under usurpation: no liability. External cause: diyah on the usurper's 'aqilah — by istihsan.

The Hanafi school addresses a nuanced scenario in tort law: a person forcibly takes a free child (ghasb), and the child dies while in the usurper's possession. The ruling distinguishes between internal and external causes of death. To justify this distinction, the Hanafi jurists employ istihsan (juristic preference), drawing an analogy to a known precedent involving a child and a deposited slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two death scenarios
The passage distinguishes between natural/internal death (fever, sudden death) and external causes (snake bite, lightning). Note the different legal consequences for each.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
For natural death: no liability (nothing due from the usurper). For external cause: blood-money (diyah) is due from the usurper's 'aqilah.
The 'aqilah are the paternal relatives or contractual guarantors who pay diyah on behalf of the liable person.
3
Identify the proof: istihsan analogy
The Hanafi school does not rely on qiyas (analogy) alone but uses istihsan. The original case: a slave deposited (wadi'ah) with a child; the child kills the slave → diyah on the child's 'aqilah.
Istihsan overrides qiyas in this case; the ruling is based on juristic preference.
4
Analyse the qiyas: asl, far', and illa
The asl (original case) is a child killing a deposited slave; the far' (new case) is the usurper's custody causing death by external means. The illa (effective cause) is the lack of direct responsibility due to the type of cause and the capacity of the person in custody.
The illa is jaliyyah (evident) and extracted by tanqih al-manat.
5
Derive the ruling for the far'
Applying the common illa, the ruling transferred is: diyah is on the usurper's 'aqilah for external deaths, just as it is on the child's 'aqilah for the killing.
This shows the Hanafi method of extending a ruling through istihsan-based qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کسی نے آزاد بچہ کو نصب کیا اور وہ بچہ غاصب کے پاس ناگہانی موت سے یا بخار سے مرگیا تو غاصب پر کچھ نہ ہوگا اور اگر سانپ کے ڈسنے اور بجلی گرنے سے مرا تو غاصب کی عاقلہ پر دیت ہے احناف کی استحسانی دلیل بچہ کے پاس غلام ودیعت رکھا گیا بچہ نے اسے قتل کر دیا تو دیت بچہ کی عاقلہ پر ہے
Someone seized a free child and that child died under the usurper by accidental death or by fever, then nothing is due from the usurper. And if he died from a snake bite or lightning strike, then blood-money (diyah) is due from the usurper's 'aqilah. The Hanafi istihsan proof: a slave was placed as a deposit with a child, and the child killed him, then blood-money is on the child's 'aqilah.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding a free child who is usurped (taken by force or seized) and then dies while in the possession of the usurper (ghasib). The ruling distinguishes between two types of death: (1) natural or internal causes like fever or sudden death—here no liability falls on the usurper because the death is not attributed to his act. (2) External harmful events like snake bite or lightning strike—these are considered injuries or causes of death that occur in the usurper's custody, so he is responsible for the diyah (blood-money), which is to be paid by his 'aqilah (his tribal or contractual guarantors). The Hanafi school supports this by istihsan (juristic preference), drawing an analogy: if a slave is placed as a trust (wadi'ah) with a child and the child kills that slave, the diyah is on the child's 'aqilah, not on the child personally, because the child is not legally responsible for intentional acts; similarly, in the usurpation case, the usurper's 'aqilah bears the diyah for external causes. This shows that the Hanafi school considers the cause of death and the capacity of the person in custody.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Wrongful seizure or usurpation of another's property or person without right.
عاقلہ ('aqilah): The paternal relatives or contractual guarantors who are liable to pay blood-money (diyah) on behalf of a person who causes death or injury.
دیت (diyah): Blood-money, the financial compensation paid to the heirs of a victim of homicide or injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi proof for ruling that diyah is due from the usurper's 'aqilah when a usurped free child dies from an external cause?
A) A direct Quranic verse on usurpation.
B) Qiyas to a case where a child kills a deposited slave, by istihsan.
C) The consensus of the Companions.
D) The hadith 'No harm and no reciprocating harm'.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the liability for a usurped child's death depends on the cause: natural deaths carry no liability, while external deaths obligate diyah on the usurper's 'aqilah, supported by istihsan.

Classification of Murder and Definition of Intentional Murder
Defines five types of murder and details intentional murder

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five types of murder according to the Hanafi school and define intentional murder ('amd) using the instrument-based criterion.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, intentional murder is established by the use of a lethal instrument, not by subjective intent alone.

This lesson introduces the Book of Injuries (Kitab al-Jinayat) and the Hanafi classification of murder. The author first explains the connection with the previous book on pawn (rahn), then lays out the five categories of murder that carry legal consequences. We will focus on the definition of intentional murder ('amd), where the key evidence is the use of a deadly instrument—a distinctive Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five types of murder
Extract the five categories from al-Quduri's statement: intentional ('amd), quasi-intentional (shibh 'amd), mistaken (khata'), that which runs in the course of mistake (ma ajra majra al-khata'), and murder by causation (qatl bi sabab). Note that only these five carry legal consequences like qisas, diyah, kaffarah, and disinheritance.
The commentator remarks that in his earlier work Dars Siraji he listed only four, combining the fourth into the third due to context.
2
Define jinaya
Understand the lexical and legal definition of jinaya: a prohibited act whose effect falls on life or wealth. This sets the scope of the book and links it to the previous book on pawn, as wealth is a means to protect life.
The definition is broad, encompassing both bodily harm and property damage.
3
Analyze the definition of 'amd
From the text, 'amd requires intentional striking with a weapon or something that functions like a weapon. Examples given: sharp wood, sharp reed, sharp stone, and fire. The Hanafi principle is that intent is inferred from the instrument (al-'amd yuthbatu bi al-ala al-qatilah).
The instrument must be one that normally causes death; subjective intent is not directly examined.
4
Examine the evidence
The passage cites Quran 4:93 ('And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell...'), along with various hadith and ijma' of the ummah, as proof for the sinfulness of 'amd. Recognize that the dalil is qat'i in both transmission and meaning.
The threat of Hellfire confirms that the act is haram and a major sin.

قال القتل على خمسة اوجد عمد و شبه عمد و خطا و ما اجرى مجرى الخطا والقتل بسبب والمراد بيان قتل تتعلق به الاحكام

In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. The Book of Injuries. Translation: This book is about the statement of injuries. The Book of Injuries. Explanation: Before this, the rulings of pawn were mentioned, and here the rulings of injuries are mentioned. The reason for suitability is that pawn is for the protection of wealth, and the rulings of injuries are for the protection of lives. Wealth is a means of protecting life, so the means was given precedence. Jinayat is the plural of jinaya, which is originally a verbal noun and is used in the sense of the passive participle. Lexically, jinaya refers to every bad deed, and legally, jinaya is a prohibited act whose effect falls on life or wealth. (That is, in the Book of Injuries, this is the meaning intended.) Otherwise, the injuries of the pilgrimage are not related to a person's self, yet they are still called injuries. In Majma' al-Anhur, vol. 2, p. 388, there is a detailed discussion on the reason for suitability. The five types of murder. He (al-Quduri) said: Murder is of five types: intentional, quasi-intentional, mistaken, what runs in the course of mistake, and murder by causation. The intent is to explain the murder to which rulings are attached. Translation: Al-Quduri said: Murder is of five types: intentional, quasi-intentional, mistaken, what runs in the course of mistake, and murder by causation, and the intent is to explain the murder to which rulings are attached. Explanation: The author says that the types of murder are many, but here we only mention those types from which rulings are attached: retaliation, blood money, expiation, and being deprived of inheritance. And these murders are five as mentioned, the detailed rulings of which will come. We have explained the types of murder concisely and comprehensively in Dars Siraji, but due to the context there, we did not separate 'what runs in the course of mistake' and mentioned only four types. Detail of intentional murder. He said: Intentional murder is when he intentionally strikes him with a weapon or what runs in the course of a weapon, like sharp wood, a sharp reed, a sharp stone, and fire, because 'amd is intent, and it cannot be known except by its evidence, which is the use of a killing instrument. So it is considered intentional in that case. Its consequence is sin, due to the saying of the Almighty: 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is...' the verse. And this has been stated by more than one from the Sunnah, and upon it the consensus of the ummah has been concluded.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Injuries (Kitab al-Jinayat) by first establishing the relationship between this book and the previous one on pawn (rahn): pawn protects wealth, while injuries (jinayat) protect life, and wealth is a means to protect life, hence the earlier placement. The author then defines jinaya lexically and legally: any prohibited act affecting life or property. Next, he enumerates the five categories of murder (qatl) that entail legal rulings: 1) intentional ('amd), 2) quasi-intentional (shibh 'amd), 3) mistaken (khata'), 4) what runs in the course of mistake (ma ajra majra al-khata'), and 5) murder by causation (qatl bi sabab). He clarifies that only these five types are discussed because they carry consequences such as qisas, diyah, kaffarah, and disinheritance. The commentator notes that in his earlier work 'Dars Siraji' he had only four categories, combining the fourth into the third. The passage then details intentional murder ('amd): it requires the use of a weapon or something that acts like a weapon, such as sharp wood, a sharp reed, a sharp stone, or fire. The reasoning is that 'amd (intent) is internal and can only be established through an external evidence (dalil), and that evidence is the use of a killing instrument (al-ala al-qatilah). The legal consequence (mujib) of intentional murder is sin (ithm), for which the evidence is the Quranic verse (4:93) and numerous hadith, as well as the consensus (ijma') of the ummah. This shows that in the Hanafi school, the primary criterion for 'amd is not merely the subjective intention to kill, but the objective use of an instrument that is normally lethal—a principle known as 'al-'amad yuthbatu bi al-ala al-qatilah'. The verse quoted (partial) is from Surah al-Nisa, and the ijma' confirms the sinfulness.

Key Terms
جنایت (jināyah): A prohibited act whose effect falls upon life or wealth.
قتل عمد (qatl 'amd): Intentionally striking another with a weapon or something that acts like a weapon, leading to death.
سلاح (silāḥ): A weapon; anything that can kill, including sharp objects, fire, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, intentional murder ('amd) is defined by the use of a lethal instrument, which serves as external evidence of intent, and it is a major sin proven by Quran, Sunnah, and ijma.

The Five Categories of Homicide in Hanafi Fiqh
States the five types of homicide that have legal rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five categories of homicide recognized in Hanafi fiqh and state the legal consequences attached to each.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, homicide is classified into five types, each with distinct rulings on qisas, diyah, and kaffarah.

The author begins by quoting al-Quduri's matn which enumerates five categories of killing that trigger legal rulings. These categories are essential for determining the appropriate penalty, be it qisas, diyah, or kaffarah. Understanding this classification is the first step in the fiqh of homicide.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the five names
Learn the Arabic and English names of the five categories: 'amd (intentional), shibh 'amd (semi-intentional), khata' (mistake), maa ajra majra al-khata' (what runs in the course of mistake), and qatl bi-sabab (killing by indirect cause).
These five are the only types that carry the specified rulings.
2
Define each category
Understand the definitions provided in the passage and key terms. For example, 'amd requires both intent and a lethal instrument; shibh 'amd involves intent to strike but not to kill with a non-lethal instrument.
Note the Hanafi specificity: the instrument matters.
3
Identify the legal consequences
The passage states that these five categories are those to which rulings are attached: qisas (retaliation), diyah (blood money), kaffarah (expiation), and deprivation from inheritance.
Later lessons will detail each consequence for each category.
4
Distinguish the categories
Focus on the differences between khata' and maa ajra majra al-khata'. The latter includes cases like killing while sleeping or by a minor, where the act is not even a conscious act of mistake.
Also distinguish qatl bi-sabab from direct killing: the liable party is the indirect cause.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
In Shafi'i fiqh, shibh 'amd is not a distinct category; it is subsumed under khata'. This has practical implications for diyah and qisas.
This difference is the source of legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال القتل على خمسة اوجد عمد و شبه عمد و خطا و ما اجرى مجرى الخطا والقتل بسبب والمراد بيان قتل تتعلق به الاحكام
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور قتل پانچ ختم پر ہے، عد ، شبہ عمد، خطا اور جو خط کے قائم مقام ہو ، اور قتل بسبب ، اور مراد ایسے قتل کا بیان ہے جس سے احکام متعلق ہوتے ہیں۔
تشریح مصنف فرماتے ہیں کہ قتل کی اقسام تو بہت ہیں مگر ہمیں یہاں صرف وہ تسمیں بیان کرتی ہیں جن سے یہ احکام متعلق ہوتے ہیں قصاص دیت، کفارہ، میراث سے محروم ہونا۔ اور یہ قتل پانچ ہیں جو مذکور ہوئے جن کے تفصیلی احکام آرہے ہیں، ہم نے درس سراجی میں اقسام قتل کو اختصار و جامعیت کے ساتھ بیان کر دیا ہے لیکن وہاں کی مناسبت سے قائم مقام خطا ، کو الگ سے بیان نہ کرتے ہوئے صرف چار قسمیں بیان کی ہیں۔
He said: Killing is of five types: 'amd (intentional), shibh 'amd (semi-intentional), khata' (mistake), what runs in the course of mistake, and killing by cause. And the intent is to explain killing to which rulings are attached. Translation: Al-Quduri said: Killing is of five types: intentional, semi-intentional, mistake, what stands in place of mistake, and killing by cause. And the intent is to explain such killing to which rulings are attached. Explanation: The author says: The types of killing are many, but here we only explain those types to which rulings are attached: retaliatory punishment (qisas), blood money (diyah), expiation (kaffarah), and deprivation from inheritance. And these are the five types mentioned, the detailed rulings of which are coming. We have in Dars-e-Siraji explained the types of killing with brevity and comprehensiveness, but according to the context there, we did not separately mention 'what runs in the course of mistake' and only mentioned four types.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement enumerating the five categories of homicide recognized in Hanafi fiqh for legal consequences. The author quotes Al-Quduri's matn. The five categories are: (1) 'amd (intentional killing), (2) shibh 'amd (semi-intentional), (3) khata' (mistake), (4) maa ajra majra al-khata' (what runs in the course of mistake, e.g., killing while sleeping or by a minor), and (5) qatl bi-sabab (killing by indirect cause, e.g., digging a well causing someone to fall). The commentator notes that these are the five types that trigger rulings such as qisas, diyah, kaffarah, and deprivation from inheritance. He also mentions that in his other work Dars-e-Siraji, he combined the fourth and third categories, presenting only four, but here he follows the fivefold division.

Key Terms
عمد ('amd): Intentional killing with a weapon or something that serves as a weapon.
شبه عمد (shibh 'amd): Semi-intentional killing: intending to strike but not to kill, using something not normally lethal.
خطأ (khata'): Mistake: killing without intent, e.g., shooting at game but hitting a person.
قتل بسبب (qatl bi-sabab): Killing by indirect cause, e.g., digging a well or placing a stone that causes death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recognizes five categories of homicide—'amd, shibh 'amd, khata', maa ajra majra al-khata', and qatl bi-sabab—each with distinct legal consequences for qisas, diyah, kaffarah, and inheritance.

Definition and Proofs of Intentional Killing ('Amd)
Defines 'amd and provides its evidentiary basis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'amd according to Hanafi fiqh, state its proof from Quran and ijma, and distinguish it from other killing categories.

Core Idea
Intentional killing = intent + lethal instrument; the instrument is the external evidence of intent.

This passage clarifies the Hanafi definition of intentional killing ('amd). The author explains that 'amd is not merely the presence of intent (which is internal and unobservable) but requires the use of a lethal instrument that serves as reliable evidence of that intent. The passage then provides the legal consequence (sin) and the proofs: the Quranic threat of Hell, multiple ahadith, and the consensus of the Ummah. Understanding this definition is essential for distinguishing 'amd from shibh al-'amd (quasi-intentional) and khata' (accidental killing), which carry different punishments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi definition of 'amd
Locate the two essential elements: intent to kill and the use of a weapon or equivalent lethal instrument.
The instrument must be something that typically can cause death, like a sword, sharp wood, fire, etc.
2
Understand the evidentiary role of the instrument
Since intent is internal, Hanafis rely on the external fact of using a deadly weapon as proof of intent.
If a non-lethal instrument is used in a lethal manner, it may still be considered 'amd based on context.
3
State the legal consequence of 'amd
The text says 'its consequence is sin' – meaning the killer incurs sin (ithm) and qisas (retaliation) is due, not merely diyah.
This distinguishes 'amd from shibh al-'amd where only diyah is due.
4
Identify the three proofs cited
Find the Quranic verse (4:93), the reference to ahadith, and the statement of ijma'.
The verse proves the sin; ahadith and ijma support the ruling.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Shafi'is do not require a lethal instrument; mere intent suffices for 'amd. Hanafis require the instrument as evidence.
This affects practical cases (e.g., killing with a small stick).

Source Text

قال فالعمد ماتعمد ضربه بسلاح او ما اجرى مجرى السلاح كالمحدد من الخشب وليطة القصب والمروة المحددة والنار لان العمد هو القصد ولا يوقف عليه الابدليله وهو استعمال الآلة القاتلة فكان عمدا فيه عند ذلك وموجب ذلك الماثم لقوله تعالى ومن يقتل مؤمنا متعمدا فجزاؤه الاية وقد نطق به غير واحد من السنة وعليه انعقد اجماع الأمة
ترجمه قدوری نے فرمایا پس قتل عمد وہ ہے کہ ارادہ کیا جائے مقتول کے مارنے کا ہتھیار سے یا اس چیز سے جو ہتھیار کے قائم مقام ہو جیسے دھار دار لکڑی اور ٹرکل کا چھلکا اور دھار دار پتھر اور آگے، اس لئے کہ عہد و دارادہ ہے جس پر بغیر اس کی دلیل کے اطلاع نہ ہو سکے گی ۔ اور وہ دلیل عمد قتل کرنے والے ہتھیار کا استعمال ہے تو وہ اس وقت قتل میں تعمد کرنے والا ہو گا اور قتل عمد کا موجب گناہ ہے۔ اللہ تعالیٰ کے فرمان من يقتل مؤمنا متعمدا فجزاءه جهنم .... الآیۃ کی وجہ سے اور اس کے سلسلہ میں ( قتل میں گناہ کے سلسلہ میں ) بہت سی حدیثیں ناطق ہیں اور اس کے اوپر امت کا اجماع منعقد ہو چکا ہے۔
تشریح پہلے مصنف نے قتل کی پانچ قسمیں بیان فرمائیں۔ یہاں سے قتل عمد کا تفصیلی ذکر ہے۔ تو فرمایا کہ جب کسی انسان کو مارنے کا ارادہ ہو اور اسکے لئے ایسا ہتھیار استعمال کیا جائے جو قتل کرنے والا ہے تو یہ قتل عمد ہے جیسے تلوار، ریوالور، بندوق، توپ وغیرہ ، اور جیسے دھار دار لکڑی اور دھار دار بانس کا چھلکا اور دھار دار پتھر اور جیسے آگ ۔ وجہ اس کی یہ ہے کہ قتل عمد کے لئے قتل کا ارادہ درکار ہے۔ ارادہ ایک مخفی اور پوشیدہ چیز ہے لہذا جب ارادہ قتل کی دلیل پائی جائے گی تو اسی پر اکتفا کر لیا جائے گا اور اس کو قتل عمد کہا جائے گا ۔ اور جب قاتل نے ایسے آلات و ہتھیار استعمال کئے جن سے قتل واقع ہوتا ہے تو یہ عمد کی دلیل ہوگی ۔ اور قتل عمد کا حکم یہ ہے کہ اس میں دیت اور کفارہ لازم نہیں آتا بلکہ گناہ اور قصاص لازم آتا ہے اس کو مصنف نے فرمایا کہ قتل عمد کا موجب گناہ ہے اب فرمائیں گے کہ قتل عمد سے گناہ ہوتا ہے اس کی دلیل ایک تو یہ آیت ہے وَمَنْ يَقْتُلْ مُؤْمِنًا مُتَعَمِّدًا فَجَزَاءُ هُ جَهَنَّمَ خَالِدًا فِيهَا وَغَضِبَ اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَ لَعَنَهُ وَ أَعَدَّ لَهُ عَذَابًا عَظِيمًا “ اس آیت سے معلوم ہوا کہ قتل مؤمن گناہ ہے۔ نیز بہت سی احادیث سے قتل کا گناہ ہونا معلوم ہے نیز قتل عمد کے گناہ ہونے پر امت کا اجماع ہے۔
He said: Intentional (killing) is that he intends to strike him with a weapon or something that takes the place of a weapon, like a sharp piece of wood, a reed peel, a sharp stone, and fire, because intentional is the intention, and it cannot be known except by its evidence, and that evidence is the use of a lethal instrument. So he is considered intentional in it at that time. And its consequence is sin, due to the saying of Allah Almighty: 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is...' the verse. And many ahadith have spoken about it, and upon it the consensus of the Ummah has been formed.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling and proof for 'amd (intentional killing). The author defines 'amd as killing where the killer intended to strike the victim with a weapon or an object equivalent to a weapon, such as a sharpened piece of wood, a sharp reed peel, a sharp stone, or fire. The rationale is that 'amd requires intent (qasd), which is internal and cannot be directly observed. Therefore, the law relies on external evidence of intent: the use of a lethal instrument. If a person uses an instrument that is typically lethal, the killing is presumed intentional. The legal consequence of 'amd is sin (ithm), not merely liability for blood money or expiation. This is proven by three evidences: (1) The Quranic verse 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell...' (Quran 4:93), (2) multiple ahadith (mutawatir in meaning), and (3) the consensus (ijma') of the Muslim ummah. The commentator clarifies that in 'amd, diyah and kaffarah are not obligatory; rather qisas (retaliation) and sin are incurred. This is a key distinction: for 'amd, the primary punishment is qisas, not financial compensation.

Key Terms
عمد ('amd): Intentional killing defined by the combination of intent to kill and use of a lethal instrument.
سلاح (silah): Weapon; includes any instrument that can cause death, such as sword, gun, sharp wood, fire.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence that intentional killing incurs sin according to this passage?
A) Consensus of the Companions
B) The Quranic verse threatening Hell for intentional killing of a believer
C) The hadith 'al-'amdu qawad'
D) Analogy to theft

Key Takeaway
Intentional killing ('amd) in Hanafi fiqh requires both intent and a lethal instrument, proven primarily by the Quranic threat of Hell, multiple ahadith, and consensus.

Qisas Limited to Intentional Killing
Ruling with proof: qisas limited to intentional killing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that qisas is obligatory only for intentional killing, cite the Quranic verse and Prophetic hadith as evidence, and explain the role of the hadith in restricting the general command.

Core Idea
Qisas is wajib only for intentional killing; the hadith restricts the general Quranic command.

In this passage from al-Hidayah, the author establishes that the obligation of qisas (retaliation) is not for every death but specifically for intentional homicide. The proof is the Quranic verse 'Prescribed for you is retaliation in cases of killing' (2:178), but a question arises: the verse is general. The answer is provided by the hadith 'al-'amdu qawad' (intentional killing entails retaliation), which restricts the general command. This lesson explores that relationship and the rationale behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its hukm
The act is qisas (retaliation) for killing. The hukm is that it is obligatory (wajib) when the killing is intentional ('amd).
2
Locate the primary dalil
The Quranic verse (2:178) is the primary evidence for qisas in cases of killing, but it is general in scope.
3
Apply the restricting Hadith
The hadith 'al-'amdu qawad' specifies that qisas is only for intentional killing, thus restricting the general quranic command.
The hadith functions as takhsis (specification) of the general verse.
4
Understand the wisdom behind the restriction
The purpose of qisas is deterrence (zajr), which is fully achieved only when the act is intentional and deliberate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال والقود لقوله تعالى كتب عليكم القصاص في القتلى الا انه تقيد بوصف العمدية لقوله عليه السلام "العمد قود أى موجبه ولان الجناية بها تتكامل وحكمة الزجر عليها تتوفر والعقوبة المتناهية لا شرع لها دون ذلک
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور قصاص اللہ تعالیٰ کے فرمان مکتب عَلَيْكُمُ الْقِصَاصُ فِى الْقَتلى کی وجہ سے مگر قصاص عدیت کے وصف کے ساتھ مقید ہے، نبی علیہ السلام کے فرمان کی وجہ سے الحمـد قـود ( مد قصاص ہے ) یعنی عمد کا موجب و حکم قصاص ہے اور اس لئے کہ عمدیت کی وجہ سے جنایت کامل ہو جاتی ہے اور زجر کی حکمت عمدیت پر پوری ہوتی ہے اور آخری درجہ کی عقوبت (سزا ) اس کے لئے قصاص کے علاوہ کوئی چیز مشروع نہیں ہے۔
تشریح گناہ کے ساتھ ساتھ قتل عمد میں قصاص بھی واجب ہوتا ہے کیونکہ قرآن کریم کی یہ آیت كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الْقِصَاصُ فِي الْقَتلى وجوب قصاص پر دال ہے۔ سوال آیت میں تو قتل عمد کا ذکر نہیں بلکہ مطلق قتل کا ذکر ہے۔ جواب ایک حدیث نے اس آیت کی تفسیر فرمادی کہ یہاں قتل سے قتل عمد مراد ہے۔ حدیث میں ہے العمد فو ذکر قتل عمد قصاص ہے یعنی قصاص کا سبب ہے۔
He said: And retaliation (qawad) due to His saying: 'Prescribed for you is retaliation in cases of killing.' But it is restricted by the description of intent due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'Intentional (killing) is retaliation', i.e., its cause. And because the crime is complete only through intent, and the wisdom of deterrence is fully realized through it, and there is no legislation for ultimate punishment other than that.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling that qisas (retaliation) is obligatory only in intentional killing ('amd). The primary proof is the Quranic verse 'Prescribed for you is retaliation in cases of killing' (Quran 2:178). However, a question arises: the verse mentions killing in general ('al-qatla'), not specifically intentional. The answer is provided by the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Al-'amdu qawad' (Intentional killing entails retaliation). This hadith restricts the general command of the Quran to intentional killing only. The commentator explains that the wisdom behind qisas is deterrence (zajr), and the most complete deterrence is achieved when the crime is intentional. Moreover, for intentional killing, there is no prescribed punishment less than qisas except diyah if the heirs forgive, but the maximum prescribed punishment is qisas. This shows that qisas is the ultimate worldly punishment for 'amd.

Key Terms
قود (qawad): Retaliation (qisas) in cases of intentional killing.
قصاص (qisas): Retaliatory punishment, typically equal retaliation (life for life).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the hadith 'al-'amdu qawad' function in relation to the Quranic verse 'kutiba alaykum al-qisas fi al-qatla'?
A) It abrogates the verse entirely
B) It restricts the verse to intentional killing only
C) It provides an alternative punishment
D) It adds a requirement of equality in faith

Key Takeaway
Qisas is wajib only for intentional killing, as the hadith 'al-'amdu qawad' restricts the general Quranic command to protect against unjust application.

Shibh 'Amd: Criterion of Weapon vs. Lethality
Definition of shibh 'amd with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's definitions of shibh 'amd, and classify a killing as 'amd or shibh 'amd based on the instrument used.

Core Idea
The instrument's legal classification (weapon vs. non-weapon) or its customary lethality decides whether killing is 'amd or shibh 'amd.

In Hanafi fiqh, the distinction between intentional murder ('amd) and quasi-intentional murder (shibh 'amd) hinges on the instrument used. This passage from al-Quduri presents two definitions within the Hanafi school: one by Imam Abu Hanifa, and another by the two disciples (the Sahibayn), which also aligns with Imam al-Shafi'i. Understanding this ikhtilaf is essential for correctly applying qisas (retaliation) and diyah (blood money).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
List clearly Abu Hanifa's definition (non-weapon or its substitute) and the Sahibayn's definition (instrument that does not normally kill).
2
Analyze the key terms
Examine 'silah' (weapon) and 'ma ajra majrahu' (substitute for a weapon) in Abu Hanifa's view. Also note 'yaqtulu ghaliban' (normally kills) in the Sahibayn's view.
These terms determine the classification.
3
Understand the underlying reasoning (talil)
The Sahibayn reason that with a normally deadly instrument, only killing can be intended, so it is 'amd. With a small instrument, discipline may be intended, so it is shibh 'amd. Abu Hanifa relies on the literal categorization of the instrument as a weapon or not.
This difference in talil leads to different rulings for the same instrument.
4
Apply to specific examples
Classify a large stone, a small rod, and a thick stick under both views. See the thamarah al-khilaf.
For Abu Hanifa: all non-weapons are shibh 'amd. For Sahibayn: large stone and thick stick are 'amd; small rod is shibh 'amd.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Recall that 'amd necessitates qisas (retaliation), while shibh 'amd requires diyah (blood money). The mufta bihi in later Hanafi practice is often the Sahibayn's view.
The consequence affects the killer's punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وشبه العمد عند ابي حنيفة ان يتعمد الضرب بما ليس بسلاح ولا ما اجرى مجرى السلاح وقال ابو يوسف و محمد وهو قول الشافعى اذا ضربه بحجر عظيم او بخشبة عظيمة فهو عمد وشبه العمد ان يتعمد ضربه بما لا يقتل به غالبا لانه يتقاصر معنى العمدية باستعمال الة صغيرة لا يقتل بها غالبا لما انه يقصد بها غیره كالتاديب ونحوه فكان شبه العمد ولا يتقاصر باستعمال آلة لا تلبث لا نه لا يقصد به الا القتل كالسيف فكان عمدا موجبا للقود ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور شبہ عمد ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک یہ ہے کہ قاتل ایسی چیز سے مارنے کا قصد کر لے جو نہ ہتھیار ہے اور نہ ہتھیار کے قائم مقام ہے اور فرمایا ابو یوسف اور محمد نے اور یہی شافعی کا قول ہے جب قاتل نے مقتول کو بھاری پتھر یا بڑی لکڑی سے مارا تو وہ عمد ہے اور شبہ عمد یہ ہے کہ مقتول کو ایسی چیز سے مارنے کا قصد کرے جس سے غالباً قتل نہیں کیا جاتا۔ اس لئے کہ عمدیت کے معنی قاصر ہو جائیں گے ایسے چھوٹے آلہ کے استعمال سے جس سے غائب قتل نہیں کیا جاتا اس لئے کہ چھوٹے آلہ سے غیر قتل کا قصد کیا جاتا ہے، جیسے تادیب اور اس کے مثل تو یہ ( آلہ صغیرہ سے قتل ) قتل شبہ عمد ہوگا اور (عمدیت کے معنی ) قاصر نہ ہوں گے ایسے آلہ کے استعمال سے جو نہ ٹھہرے اس لئے کہ اس سے قتل ہی کا ارادہ ہوتا ہے جیسے تلوار تویہ قتل عمد ہو گا جو قصاص کو واجب کرے گا۔ تشریح یہاں سے قتل شبہ عمد کا بیان ہے۔ امام ابو حنیفہ فرماتے ہیں کہ جب قصد آمارے اور ایسی چیز سے مارے جو ہتھیار شمار نہیں ہوتا تو وہ شبہ عمد ہے خواہ اس چیز کو استعمال کرنے سے عموما موت واقع ہو جاتی ہو یا نہ ہوتی ہو لہذا لاٹھی سے مارے یا پتھر سے مارے یا چھڑی سے مارے یا قینچی سے مارے چونکہ یہ ہتھیار شار نہیں ہوتے اس لئے یہ تمام صورتیں شبہ عمد کے اندر داخل ہیں ۔ صاحبین اور امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ جس چیز سے عموما قتل نہ کیا جاتا ہو اس سے قصد آمار نے سے شبہ عمد ہوگا ورنہ اگر چہ ہتھیار نہ ہو لیکن اس کے استعمال سے موت واقع ہو جاتی ہو تو ایسی چیز سے مارنے سے قتل عمد ہوگا۔ لہذا صاحبین کے نزدیک لاٹھی وغیرہ سے اگر مارا تو بھی قتل عمد ہوگا اور بھاری پتھر سے اگر مارا تب بھی قتل عمد ہو گا البتہ اگر چھڑی سے مارا تو شبہ عمد ہے لیکن اگر لگا تار چھڑی ماری تو پھر قتل عمد ہو جائے گا۔ خلاصہ کلام ان دونوں مذہبوں میں عموم خصوص مطلق کی نسبت ہے، یعنی صاحبین کے نزدیک جو شبہ عمدہ ہے وہ امام صاحب کے نزدیک بھی شبہ عمد ہے اور جو امام صاحب کے نزدیک شبہ عمد ہے صاحبین کے نزدیک اس کا شبہ عمد ہونا ضروری نہیں ہے بلکہ وہ قتل عمد بھی ہو۔ صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ جب چھوٹا سا آلہ مثلاً چھڑی استعمال کی تو چونکہ اس سے عموما قتل واقع نہیں ہوتا اس لئے اس میں تادیب و تنبیہ مقصود ہوتی ہے تو یہ قتل شبہ عمد ہوگا۔ بغیر نہیں چھوڑتا جیسے تلوار وغیرہ تو ایسا قتل قتل عمد ہوگا اور یہاں قصاص واجب ہوگا۔
He said: And shibh 'amd according to Abu Hanifa is that he intentionally strikes with something that is not a weapon and not something that takes the place of a weapon. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said - and it is the saying of al-Shafi'i - when he strikes him with a large stone or a large piece of wood, it is 'amd. And shibh 'amd is that he intentionally strikes him with something that does not normally kill, because the meaning of 'amdiyyah (intentionality) is diminished by using a small instrument that does not normally kill, since he intends something else by it, like discipline and the like, so it is shibh 'amd. And it is not diminished by using an instrument that does not stay (i.e., a weapon that does not linger, like a sword), because he intends nothing but killing with it, like a sword, so it is 'amd that necessitates qisas. Translation: al-Quduri said: And shibh 'amd according to Abu Hanifa is that the killer intends to strike with something that is neither a weapon nor a substitute for a weapon. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said - and this is also the saying of al-Shafi'i - when the killer strikes the victim with a heavy stone or a large piece of wood, that is 'amd. And shibh 'amd is that he intends to strike him with something that does not normally kill. Because the meaning of intentionality is diminished by the use of a small instrument with which killing is not normally done, since with a small instrument he intends something other than killing, like discipline and the like, so it is shibh 'amd. And it (the meaning of intentionality) is not diminished by the use of an instrument that does not stay (i.e., a lethal weapon), because he intends nothing but killing with it, like a sword, so it is 'amd that necessitates qisas. Commentary: From here, the description of shibh 'amd is given. Imam Abu Hanifa says that when he intends to strike and strikes with something that is not counted as a weapon, it is shibh 'amd, whether or not death generally occurs from using that object. Hence, whether he strikes with a stick, a stone, a small rod, or scissors, since these are not weapons in the Shari'ah, all these cases fall under shibh 'amd. The Sahibayn and Imam al-Shafi'i say: When the instrument is one with which killing is not normally done, then intentional striking with it is shibh 'amd; otherwise, even if it is not a weapon but death occurs through its use, then striking with such an object is 'amd murder. Therefore, according to the Sahibayn, if he strikes with a stick etc., it is 'amd murder; if he strikes with a heavy stone, it is also 'amd murder. However, if he strikes with a small rod, it is shibh 'amd. But if he strikes repeatedly with the rod, then it becomes 'amd murder. Summary: Between these two positions, there is a relationship of absolute generality and specificity. That is, what is shibh 'amd according to the Sahibayn is also shibh 'amd according to Imam Abu Hanifa. But what is shibh 'amd according to Imam Abu Hanifa is not necessarily shibh 'amd according to the Sahibayn; it could be 'amd. The Sahibayn say that when a small instrument, like a rod, is used, since it does not normally cause death, the intention is discipline and admonition, so it is shibh 'amd. As for an instrument that does not fail to kill, like a sword, such killing is 'amd and qisas becomes obligatory.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (also involving Imam al-Shafi'i) on the definition of shibh 'amd (quasi-intentional murder). The matn is from al-Quduri's al-Mukhtasar, and the sharh expands on the ikhtilaf. The core issue: what instrument distinguishes 'amd from shibh 'amd? According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the criterion is whether the instrument is a weapon (silah) or something that serves as a weapon (ma ajra majra al-silah). If it is not a weapon, the killing is shibh 'amd, regardless of whether the object is deadly (like a large stone or stick). So for Abu Hanifa, any strike with a non-weapon object is shibh 'amd, provided there was intent to strike (but not necessarily intent to kill). According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and also Imam al-Shafi'i, the criterion is whether the instrument normally kills (meaning it is the type of object that is generally lethal, like a large stone or stick). If the object is such that it usually causes death, the killing is 'amd, even if it is not a weapon. If the object is small and not normally lethal (like a small rod), then it is shibh 'amd. The commentary explains the practical difference: for Abu Hanifa, a stick and a large stone are both non-weapons, so both are shibh 'amd. For the Sahibayn, a large stone and a thick stick are 'amd because they are deadly by custom, while a small rod is shibh 'amd. The commentary notes a summary: what Sahibayn consider shibh 'amd is also shibh 'amd for Abu Hanifa, but what Abu Hanifa considers shibh 'amd may be 'amd for the Sahibayn. The underlying reasoning (talil) is that with deadly instruments, the only possible intent is killing (hence 'amd), while with small instruments, the intent could be discipline, so the killing is quasi-intentional. The ruling also affects qisas: 'amd necessitates qisas (retaliation), while shibh 'amd does not (instead blood money is due).

Key Terms
شبه عمد (shibh 'amd): Quasi-intentional killing: striking with intent but without the instrument being typically lethal, often with the intent to discipline rather than kill.
سلاح (silah): Weapon; something designed for killing, like a sword, spear, gun, etc. Also includes things that take its place (ma ajra majrahu).
قود (qawad): Qisas (retaliation) in kind for murder or injury; specifically here, the death penalty for the killer.
آلة لا تلبث (alah la talbuth): An instrument that does not stay; i.e., a weapon that kills immediately, like a sword, as opposed to a slow-acting instrument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), which of the following would be classified as shibh 'amd?
A) Striking with a sword
B) Striking with a large stone
C) Striking with a small rod intended for discipline
D) Striking with a spear

Key Takeaway
The instrument determines the category: Abu Hanifa uses weapon status; the Sahibayn use customary lethality; the mufta bihi follows the Sahibayn.

The Legal Consequences of Shibh al-Amd: Sin, Kaffarah, and Heavy Diyah
The legal consequences of shibh al-amd: sin, expiation, and heavy diyah on the ʿāqila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three consequences of shibh al-amd (sin, kaffarah, heavy diyah), explain the reason for each, and identify that the heavy diyah is wajib upon the ʿāqila in three years.

Core Idea
Shibh al-amd combines intent to strike (sin) with resemblance to accident (kaffarah); the heavy diyah is paid by the clan over three years.

This lesson unpacks the ruling of shibh al-amd (semi-intentional killing) according to the Hanafi school. The passage lays out the three consequences that become wajib: sin, expiation (kaffarah), and heavy diyah. It also introduces a foundational principle for determining diyah liability and specifies the payment period based on the practice of ʿUmar ibn al-Khattab. Understanding this ruling is crucial for distinguishing shibh al-amd from both intentional (ʿamd) and accidental (khaṭā) killing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three consequences
Read the passage and list the three things that become wajib in shibh al-amd: sin (ithm), expiation (kaffarah), and heavy diyah (diyah mughtallaẓah). Note the reasons given for each.
The sin is due to the killer's intent to strike; kaffarah is due to resemblance to accidental killing; heavy diyah is wajib on the ʿāqila.
2
Understand the basis for kaffarah
Analyze why kaffarah is wajib. The text says 'because it resembles accidental killing.' In khaṭā, kaffarah is wajib according to the Qur'an (4:92). Here, the resemblance triggers the same obligation, even though the killing is not purely accidental.
This shows that shibh al-amd shares the attribute of lack of intent to kill with khaṭā, hence the kaffarah.
3
Learn the general principle for diyah liability
The passage states a principle: any diyah that becomes obligatory directly from the act of killing (ibitidā') is upon the ʿāqila, not the killer. This is by analogy to accidental killing. Understand the distinction: if the diyah arises from a later cause (e.g., sulh), it is on the killer.
This principle is derived from the practice of the Prophet and the Companions.
4
Note the payment period and its proof
The diyah is payable in three years. The text cites the judgment (qaḍiyyah) of ʿUmar ibn al-Khattab. Recognize that this is a proof (dalīl) from the practice of a Companion, which is accepted in Hanafi usul.
The three-year period allows the ʿāqila to pay in installments, alleviating financial burden.
5
Recognize the heavy diyah and its deferred detail
The diyah is described as 'heavy' (mughtallaẓah). The author promises to define the specifics later. For now, understand that heavy diyah involves camels of certain qualities (e.g., pregnant she-camels) and is distinct from the standard diyah for accidental killing.
This heavy diyah is the same as for intentional killing in amount but differs in payment and liability.

He said: The consequence of that, according to both opinions, is sin because it is killing while intent on striking, and expiation (kaffarah) due to its resemblance to accidental killing, and a heavy diyah upon the ʿāqila. The principle is that any diyah that becomes obligatory initially by the killing, not by a meaning that occurs later, is upon the ʿāqila by analogy to accidental killing. It becomes payable in three years due to the judgment of ʿUmar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him), and it is heavy, and we will later describe the nature of the heaviness, Allah willing.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage outlines the legal consequences (mūjib) of shibh al-amd. The author (al-Quduri) states that it incurs sin because the killer intended the striking, and expiation (kaffarah) because it resembles accidental killing (khaṭā). The diyah is heavy (mughtallaẓah) and is due upon the ʿāqila (the killer's clan). The principle (qa'idah) is that if the diyah becomes obligatory directly from the act of killing (ibitidā'), it is on the ʿāqila. If it arises from a later cause, like sulh (settlement), it is on the killer personally. The diyah is payable in three annual installments, based on the practice of ʿUmar ibn al-Khattab. The author promises to explain the nature of 'heavy' diyah later. The commentary clarifies that this is the ruling according to both schools (probably referring to both Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, as they agree on these consequences).

Key Terms
العاقلة (al-ʿāqila): The male agnatic relatives of the killer (on the father's side) who are liable to pay the diyah.
دية مغلظة (diyah mughtallaẓah): A heavy diyah, which for shibh al-amd is one hundred camels with certain qualities (to be explained later).
الاثم (al-ithm): Sin – the moral and legal culpability incurred by the killer for intentionally striking.
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation – an act of worship (e.g., freeing a slave, fasting two months) required to atone for the killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the killer is sinful.

D) Because the killer made a settlement with the family.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, shibh al-amd results in sin (due to intent to strike), kaffarah (due to resemblance to accident), and a heavy diyah payable by the ʿāqila in three annual installments.

Qatl Khata by Cause: The Sleeper and Causal Homicide
Defining acts treated as khata in law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between direct accidental homicide (khata) and causal homicide (qatl bi-sabab) and state the Hanafi rulings on diyah, kaffarah, and inheritance for causal homicide.

Core Idea
In causal homicide with transgression, diyah is wajib on the aqilah but no kaffarah or inheritance deprivation applies.

This passage from Al-Hidayah (via Al-Quduri) identifies two scenarios that are legally treated as accidental homicide: a sleeper rolling onto someone, and causal acts like digging a well or placing a stone on another's land. The key condition is that the actor is transgressive (mut'addi), which triggers diyah liability but not expiation or inheritance disqualification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage first mentions the sleeper rolling onto another, then discusses causal homicide (qatl bi-sabab). Note that both are treated as khata in law, but their consequences differ slightly.
2
Analyze the condition of transgression
The causal homicide scenario requires that the act be done on non-owned land (ta'addi). This transgression elevates the cause to the level of a direct act for financial liability.
Without transgression (e.g., digging on own land), the ruling may differ.
3
Determine the legal consequences
For causal homicide with transgression: diyah is wajib on the aqilah, no kaffarah is required, and the killer does not lose inheritance from the victim.
These consequences are contrasted with direct khata where kaffarah and inheritance deprivation apply.
4
Compare with direct khata
Direct accidental homicide (e.g., hunting accident) incurs diyah on aqilah AND kaffarah (expiation) and inheritance deprivation. Causal homicide with transgression only has diyah.
The difference stems from the absence of direct killing (mubasharah) in causal cases.

Source Text
