Forgiving a Cut Does Not Forgive Resulting Death
Imam Abu Hanifa refutes Sahibayn's analogy regarding saraaya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Imam Abu Hanifa holds that forgiving a cut does not automatically waive the diya for death, and distinguish it from forgiving a generic injury or explicit mention.

Core Idea
Forgiving the act that causes death does not forgive the death itself unless the consequence is explicitly included.

In this passage, Imam Abu Hanifa responds to the argument of his two leading students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). They claimed that if a victim forgives a cut that later leads to death, the forgiveness should cover the death as well. Imam refutes this by drawing a sharp distinction between the cut itself and the lethal consequence. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to analyze how legal consequences hinge on precise wording of forgiveness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core disagreement
The Sahibayn argue that since the cut spreads to cause death (saraaya), it is a type of cut, so forgiving the cut should cover the death. Imam rejects this categorization.
2
Analyze Imam's redefinition of saraaya
Imam says the spreading cut (saari) is actually killing from the beginning, not merely a cut. As a cut, it has no present legal consequence because the consequence is death.
The cut's ruling is 'suspended' until the outcome is known.
3
Apply the principle of forgiveness scope
A person can only forgive his own right. Here the victim's right at the moment of forgiveness is the diya for the imminent death (since the cut will likely kill), not the cut itself. So forgiving 'the cut' does not waive the diya.
The cut had no independent legal effect at that point.
4
Distinguish from generic injury and explicit wording
Imam contrasts with forgiving 'an injury' (jinaayah), which is a generic term covering all types, and forgiving a head wound (shajjah) which explicitly includes consequences. Explicit wording like 'I forgive the cut and all its effects' does waive the death.
The key is the wording: specific vs. generic vs. explicit.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the victim forgives the cut and later dies, under Imam's view the diya is still owed to the heirs. Under Sahibayn's view, it would be waived. The mufta bihi is Imam's position.
This affects whether the heirs can claim blood money.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الجنايات
۹۸
اشرف الهدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۵
تو اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ اس نے قطع کو معاف کیا تھا۔ اور یہاں یہ ظاہر ہوا قتل تو حق کچھ ہے اور معافی دوسری چیز کی ہے حالانکہ یہ اصول مسلم ہے کہ آدمی اپنا حق ہی معاف کر سکتا ہے اور اس کا حق جس چیز میں ہے وہ اس نے معاف کیا لہذا قطع کو معاف کرنے سے قتل کا موجب یعنی دیت معاف نہ ہوگی۔
امام صاحب کی طرف سے صاحبین کی دلیل کا جواب
ولا نسلم ان السارى نوع من القطع وان السراية صفة له بل السارى قتل من الابتداء وكذا لا موجب له من حيث كونه قطعا فلا يتناوله العفو بخلاف العفو عن الجناية لانه اسم جنس وبخلاف العفو عن الشجة وما دث منها لان صريح فى العفو عن السراية والقتل
ترجمہ اور ہم یہ بات تسلیم نہیں کرتے کہ ساری قطع کی ایک قسم ہے اور ہم یہ تسلیم نہیں کرتے کہ سرایت قطع کی صفت ہے بلکہ قطع سناری شروع ہی سے قتل ہے اور ایسے ہی قطع ید کا قطع ہونے کی حیثیت سے (اب) کوئی موجب نہیں ہے تو قطع کو معافی شامل نہ ہوگی بخلاف جنایت سے معاف کر دینے کے اس لئے کہ جنایت اسم جنس ہے اور بخلاف سر کے زخم کو معاف کر دینے کے اور اس چیز کو معاف کر دینے کے جو اس زخم سے پیدا ہو اس لئے کہ یہ صراحة سرایت اور قتل کو معاف کر دینا ہے۔ تشریح یہ امام صاحب کی طرف سے صاحبین کی دلیل کا جواب ہے ، فرماتے ہیں اے صاحبین ! کیا فرمایا آپ نے کہ قطع کی دو قسمیں ہیں ، ساری اور مختصر ہم اس کو تسلیم نہیں کرتے اور نہ ہم یہ تسلیم کرتے ہیں کہ سرایت قطع کی صفت ہے بلکہ قطع ساری تو شروع ہی سے قتل ہے اگر چہ ظہور اس کا اب ہوا ہے۔ اسی طرح جب قطع کے بعد زخم سرایت کر کے نوبت موت تک پہنچ جائے تو اب قطع کا کوئی حکم نہیں رہا بلکہ اب تو قتل کا حکم جاری ہوگا یعنی دیت واجب ہو گی ۔ تو اس کا حق دیت ہے اور اس نے معاف کیا ہے قطع کو اور قطع کا ابھی کوئی حکم نہیں تھا تو معافی اپنے محل پر نہ رہی۔ اس وجہ سے قطع کے عفو سے قتل کی معافی نہ ہوگی۔
اور اے صاحبین آپ نے جو جنایت سے استدلال کیا ہے یہ میچ نہیں۔ اس لئے کہ جنایت اسم جنس ہے جو جنایت ساریہ اور مقتصر و دونوں کو شامل ہے۔ اس لئے اگر جنایت کو معاف کیا ہو تو دونوں صورتیں معاف ہو جائیں گی ، اور قطع کو معاف کرنے سے قتل معاف نہ ہو گا اور اگر خالد نے صراحت کر دی ہو کہ میں نے قطع کو اور اس سے پیدا ہونے والے تمام اثرات کو معاف کیا تو اب قتل بھی معاف ہو جائے گا کیونکہ اب اس نے صراحت معاف کر دیا ہے یعنی قطع سے بھی اور سرایت سے بھی اور قتل سے بھی ۔
This is the answer: He had forgiven the cutting. And here it became clear that the killing is a different right, and the forgiveness is for something else. While it is an established principle that a person can only forgive his own right, and his right is in the thing he forgave. Therefore, forgiving the cut does not forgive the consequence of the killing, i.e., the blood money. The Imam's response to the Sahibayn's proof: And we do not concede that the spreading (sari) is a type of cutting, nor that the spreading is a description of it; rather, the spreading is killing from the beginning. Likewise, it has no legal consequence as a cutting, so forgiveness does not include it. Unlike forgiving an injury (jinaayah) because it is a generic noun, and unlike forgiving a head wound (shajjah) and what results from it because that is explicit in forgiving the spreading and killing. Translation: And we do not accept that the sari is a type of cutting, nor that the saraaya is a description of the cut. Rather, the cutting that spreads is killing from the very beginning. Similarly, the cutting of the hand, as a cutting, has no [current] legal consequence, so forgiveness does not include it. Unlike forgiving an injury, because injury is a generic term, and unlike forgiving a head wound and what results from it, because that explicitly forgives the spreading and killing. Explanation: This is the Imam's response to the Sahibayn's proof. He says: O Sahibayn! You said that there are two types of cutting: spreading and non-spreading. We do not accept that, nor do we accept that spreading is a description of the cut. Rather, the spreading cut is killing from the beginning, even if its manifestation occurs later. Similarly, when after the cut the wound spreads and leads to death, the ruling of cutting no longer remains; rather, the ruling of killing applies, i.e., blood money becomes obligatory. So his right is blood money, and he has forgiven the cutting, and the cutting had no ruling at that time, so the forgiveness did not fall upon its proper place. For this reason, forgiving the cut does not forgive the killing. And O Sahibayn, your deduction from 'injury' is not correct, because injury is a generic term including both spreading and non-spreading injuries. Therefore, if injury is forgiven, both forms are forgiven. But forgiving the cut does not forgive the killing. However, if Khalid explicitly says: 'I forgive the cut and all its effects,' then the killing is also forgiven, because he explicitly forgave the cut, the spreading, and the killing.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) had argued that cutting a hand that leads to death (saraaya) is a type of cutting, so forgiving the cut should also forgive the resulting death. Imam Abu Hanifa refutes this. He argues that the spreading cut is essentially killing from the outset, not merely a cut. Therefore, when the victim forgives 'the cutting,' he forgives only the act of cutting, which at that moment has no legal consequence (because the consequence is death, not the cut itself). The forgiveness does not extend to the blood money (diya) due for the death. Imam distinguishes this from forgiving 'an injury' (jinaayah), which is a generic term covering all types, and from forgiving a specific head wound (shajjah) which explicitly includes its consequences. The key principle: a person can only forgive his own right; here the right at the time of forgiveness is the blood money for imminent death, not the cut itself. If the victim explicitly forgives 'the cut and all its resultant effects,' then the death is also forgiven. The passage concludes by noting that explicit wording can cover both.

Key Terms
سارى (saari): A cut or wound that spreads and leads to death (saraaya).
سراية (saraayah): The spreading of an injury to cause death.
قطع (qat'): Cutting off a limb (e.g., hand).
عفو (afw): Forgiveness/waiver of a legal right.
جناية (jinaayah): Injury or offense, a generic term covering all types of physical harm.
شجة (shajjah): A head wound that reaches the bone or deeper.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a victim forgives the cutting of his hand but later dies from the wound, what is the ruling according to Imam Abu Hanifa?
A) The diya for death is also waived because the cut led to death.
B) The diya for death is still due because the forgiveness only covered the cut, not the death.
C) The diya is waived only if the victim explicitly says 'I forgive the cut and all its effects.'
D) Both B and C are correct.

Key Takeaway
Forgiveness of a specific act (like a cut) does not waive liability for a later death unless the death is explicitly included in the forgiveness.

Forgiveness of Erroneous vs Intentional Injury: Effect on Estate
Imam Muhammad rules on forgiveness of erroneous vs intentional cut

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the legal effect of forgiving an intentional injury from forgiving an erroneous injury, and explain the rationale based on the nature of the obligation (qisas vs diya).

Core Idea
Forgiveness of intentional injury (qisas) uses whole estate; forgiveness of erroneous injury (diya) limited to one-third.

This passage from Imam Muhammad addresses a critical question: how does the victim's forgiveness of an injury affect his estate after his death? The answer hinges on the nature of the consequence (mujib) of the injury—whether it is qisas (retaliation) or diya (blood money). This distinction has practical implications for heirs and testamentary dispositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between intentional (amdan) and erroneous (khata) cutting of a hand. In both cases, the victim's forgiveness (afw) is valid, but the effect on the estate differs.
The type of injury determines whether the consequence is qisas or diya.
2
Understand the consequence (mujib)
For intentional injury, the consequence is qisas (retaliation), which is not property. For erroneous injury, the consequence is diya (blood money), which is property. This classification is key to the ruling.
Qisas is a personal right, not inheritable; diya is a financial right and becomes part of the estate.
3
Apply the analogy (qiyas)
Imam Muhammad draws an analogy with wills: a will to lend land (i'arah) is effective from the entire estate because it does not involve ownership transfer (like qisas). A will to give land (ownership) is limited to one-third (like diya).
This analogy clarifies why intentional forgiveness is unrestricted and erroneous forgiveness is limited.
4
Derive the ruling
Since qisas is not property, the heirs have no right to it, so forgiveness takes effect from the entire estate. Since diya is property, the heirs have a right to it, so forgiveness is like a bequest and limited to one-third.
The same principle applies whether the forgiveness happens before or after death.

What is the ruling if the hand was cut erroneously? And if the cutting was erroneous, he (Imam Muhammad) has treated it like intentional in these respects, in agreement and disagreement. His absolute statement indicates that. However, if it was erroneous, it (the forgiveness) applies to one-third of the estate; if intentional, it applies to all of the property. Because the consequence of intentional is qisas (retaliation), and the heirs have no right attached to it, since it is not wealth. So it becomes like the case where one instructs the loan of his land. As for erroneous, its consequence is wealth, and the heirs' right is attached to it, so it is considered from one-third. And if the hand was cut erroneously, Muhammad has made it analogous to intentional in all consensual and disputed aspects. His absolute statement indicates that. If the cut is erroneous, forgiveness is from one-third; if intentional, it is from the entire estate. Because the consequence of intentional is qisas, and before death the heirs have no right attached to it because qisas is not property. So it is as if he made a will to lend his land. As for erroneous, its consequence is property, and the heirs' right is attached to it, so forgiveness is considered from one-third. (The commentary then explains that this is the same for all cases of cutting, and distinguishes between intentional and erroneous based on whether the obligation is qisas or diya.)
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage addresses the effect of forgiveness (afw) when a hand is cut either intentionally (amdan) or erroneously (khata). Imam Muhammad states that the ruling is the same in terms of the basic legal effect: forgiveness is valid. However, there is a difference regarding how it affects the estate of the victim if he later dies. If the cut was intentional, the primary consequence is qisas (retaliation), which is not property. Therefore, the victim's forgiveness is akin to a disposition that does not involve the heirs' right to property. Thus, forgiveness takes effect from the entire estate (all property) because the heirs have no right to qisas. This is analogous to a will to loan (i'arah) land, which is effective from the whole estate because it is not a transfer of ownership. If the cut was erroneous, the consequence is diya (blood money), which is property. The heirs have a right to that property, so the victim's forgiveness can only affect one-third of his estate, similar to a bequest. The passage uses the example of a will to give land versus a will to lend land to illustrate the principle. The key takeaway: intentional injury yields qisas (non-property), so forgiveness is unrestricted; erroneous injury yields diya (property), so forgiveness is limited to one-third.

Key Terms
عمد (amd): Intentional act; here, intentional cutting of a hand.
خطا (khata): Erroneous/unintentional act; here, accidental cutting.
موجب (mujib): Legal consequence; what is obligated as a result.
قصاص (qisas): Retaliatory punishment; life for life, limb for limb.
دیت (diya): Blood money; financial compensation for homicide or injury.
ثلث (thuluth): One-third; the maximum portion of estate that can be disposed of by will or forgiveness of diya.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone erroneously cuts another's hand and the victim forgives the injury before dying, how much of the victim's estate is affected by this forgiveness?
A) All of the estate, because forgiveness is absolute.
B) One-third of the estate, because the consequence (diya) is property.
C) None, because forgiveness only applies to intentional injuries.
D) Half of the estate, according to general principles.

Key Takeaway
The effect of forgiveness on the estate depends on whether the injury was intentional (qisas, unrestricted) or erroneous (diya, limited to one-third).

Effect of Pardon for Intentional vs Mistaken Hand Cutting
Distinguishing effect of pardon on estate based on intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the effect of pardon on the estate for intentional vs mistaken hand cutting, and apply the principle that qisas is not property to derive the correct ruling.

Core Idea
Pardon for intentional cutting applies to the entire estate; for mistaken cutting, only to one-third.

When a victim pardons a crime before death, does the pardon affect the entire estate or just one-third? The answer depends on the nature of the right. Intentional harm triggers qisas, which is not considered property, so heirs' rights do not attach. Mistaken harm triggers diyah, which is property, so heirs' rights attach. This lesson explores that distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage discusses two cases: intentional cutting (amd) and mistaken cutting (khata). First, note the default ruling from Imam Muhammad's general statement that they are treated similarly in most aspects.
Imam Muhammad's 'itlaq suggests no difference in some rulings.
2
Locate the small difference
The commentary reveals one difference: the effect of pardon on the estate. For intentional, pardon applies to the entire estate; for mistaken, only to one-third.
This is the key distinction to remember.
3
Understand the proof (talil)
Intentional cutting necessitates qisas, which is not property (mal). Since heirs' rights attach only to property, their right does not attach to qisas. Therefore, the pardon is like a bequest of a non-property right and takes effect from the full estate.
Qisas is not considered 'mal' in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Contrast with mistaken cutting
Mistaken cutting necessitates diyah, which is property. Heirs' rights do attach to property, so the pardon is limited to one-third, just like a bequest of property.
Diyah is considered mal and thus subject to inheritance limits.
5
Apply the analogous example
The passage compares it to a will of land (property) vs a directive for ariyah (loan for use, not property). A will of land is limited to one-third; a directive for ariyah applies to the entire estate. This reinforces the distinction.
The ariyah example illustrates that non-property rights are not restricted to one-third.

Source Text

اور اگر ہاتھ کا کاٹنا خطاء ہو تو اس کو محمد نے تمام اتفاقی اور اختلافی صورتوں میں عمد کے قائم مقام کیا ہے اس کو محمد کا اطلاق بتلا رہا ہے اگر قطع خطا ہو تو معافی ثلث سے ہوگی اور اگر عمدا ہو تو معافی پورے مال سے ہوگی اس لئے کہ عمد کا موجب قصاص ہے اور قصاص قبل الموت ورثا کا حق متعلق نہیں ہوتا اس لئے کہ قصاص مال نہیں ہے تو ایسا ہو گیا جیسے اس نے وصیت کی ہو اپنی زمین کے اعادہ کی لیکن خطا تو اس کا موجب مال ہے اور ورثا کا حق مال سے متعلق ہو جاتا ہے تو معافی تہائی سے معتبر ہوگی۔ بحر صاحب ہدایہ نے اب تک جو تفصیل فرمائی ہے یہ اس صورت کی ہے جب کہ زید نے عمدا ہاتھ کاٹا ہو اب سوال پیدا ہوا کہ اگر ہاتھ خطاء کاٹا ہو تو اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ اس صورت کا بھی یہی حکم ہے ہر ہر صورت میں ۔

امام محمد کے اطلاق سے یعنی انہوں نے عمد اور خطاء کا ذکر کیئے بغیر یہ حکم بیان فرمایا ہے تو اس اطلاق سے یہ حکم سمجھے ۔
کیا عمد اور خطاء میں کہیں بھی کچھ بھی فرق نہیں ہے؟
جی ایک تھوڑا سا فرق ہے۔ اور وہ یہ ہے کہ اگر زید نے خالد کا ہاتھ خطاء کاٹا ہو اور خالد نے اس جنایت کو معاف کر دیا تو معافی تہائی مال سے معتبر ہوگی اور اگر عمداً تو پورے مال سے معتبر ہوگی۔ اور اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ عمد میں قصاص واجب ہوتا ہے اور قصاص چونکہ مال نہیں ہے لہذا اس کے ساتھ ورثہ کا حق بھی متعلق نہ ہو گا۔ جس سے یہ معافی پورے مال سے معتبر ہوگی۔
اس کی مثال بعینہ یہ ہے کہ اگر زید اپنی زمین کی وصیت کرے تو یہ وصیت ثلث میں نافذ ہوگی اور اگر اپنی زمین کے بارے میں یہ کہے کہ اس کو بکر کو عاریت پر دے دینا تو یہ وصیت پوری زمین پر نافذ ہوگی۔ کیونکہ عاریت میں مستعیر کو مالک بنانا مقصد نہیں ہے بلکہ نفع مقصود ہے تو یہ مال نہ ہوا اور ورثا کا حق مال سے وابستہ ہوتا ہے۔ لہذا پوری وصیت نافذ کر دی جائے گی۔ اور زمین کی وصیت میں مال ہے لہذا وہ صرف ثلث میں نافذ ہوگی۔ یہی حال قطع عمد کا ہے اور اگر قطع خطاء ہو تو اس کا موجب مال ہے اور مال کے ساتھ ورثا کا حق وابستہ ہوتا ہے لہذا یہ معافی ثلث سے معتبر ہوگی۔ پس عمد اور خطاء میں یہاں اتنا سا فرق ہے اور بس۔
And if the cutting of the hand is mistaken, then Muhammad has made it equivalent to intentional in all agreed and disagreed upon cases. His general application indicates this. If the cutting is mistaken, then pardon will be from one-third, and if intentional, then pardon will be from the entire wealth, because intentional cutting necessitates qisas, and qisas before death does not attach the right of the heirs because qisas is not property. So it becomes as if he had made a will regarding the return of his land. But mistaken cutting necessitates property, and the right of the heirs attaches to property, so pardon will be considered from one-third. The author of Hidayah has so far detailed this for the case when Zayd intentionally cut the hand. Now a question arises: if the hand was cut mistakenly, the answer is that the same ruling applies in every case. Where did you know this? From Imam Muhammad's general application, i.e., he stated this ruling without mentioning intentional or mistaken. Is there any difference between intentional and mistaken? Yes, a small difference. That is, if Zayd cut Khalid's hand mistakenly and Khalid pardoned this crime, then the pardon is considered from one-third of the wealth, and if intentionally, then from the entire wealth. The reason is that intentional cutting requires qisas, and since qisas is not property, the right of the heirs does not attach to it. Therefore, this pardon will be from the entire wealth. Its exact example is that if Zayd makes a will regarding his land, this will is executed from one-third, but if he says regarding his land, 'Give it to Bakr as a loan (ariyah),' then this will is executed from the entire land, because in ariyah, making the borrower owner is not intended; benefit is intended, so it is not property, and the right of the heirs is attached to property. Therefore, the entire will is executed. And in the will of land, there is property, so it is executed only from one-third. This is the case of intentional cutting. And if the cutting is mistaken, then it necessitates property, and with property the right of the heirs is attached, so this pardon is considered from one-third. Thus, this is the only difference between intentional and mistaken.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation distinguishing the effect of pardon for intentional versus mistaken cutting of a hand. The author clarifies that Imam Muhammad's general statement equating the two in some contexts does not negate a difference in the effect of pardon. The key distinction: intentional cutting triggers qisas, which is not property, so the heirs' right does not attach to it; thus, if the victim pardons before death, it is like a bequest from the entire estate. Mistaken cutting triggers diyah, which is property, so the heirs' right attaches, and the pardon is effective only up to one-third of the estate. The example of a bequest of land versus a directive for ariyah illustrates the principle: a bequest of property is limited to one-third, while a directive for a non-property benefit (like ariyah) is effective from the entire estate because it is not considered property to which heirs' rights attach.

Key Terms
عفو (‘afw): Pardon or forgiveness of the right to retaliation or compensation by the victim.
عمد (‘amd): Intentional act; here, intentional cutting of a hand.
خطأ (khata’): Mistaken or unintentional act; here, unintentional cutting of a hand.
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation in kind, specifically for intentional bodily harm or homicide.
دية (diyah): Blood money, financial compensation for homicide or bodily harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is pardon for intentional hand cutting effective from the entire estate, while pardon for mistaken cutting is limited to one-third?
A) Because intentional cutting is more severe
B) Because qisas is not property, so heirs' rights do not attach
C) Because the victim's intention changes the ruling
D) Because Imam Muhammad said so

Key Takeaway
The effect of pardon on the estate depends on whether the right is qisas (non-property) or diyah (property), resulting in full effect for intentional cutting and one-third for mistaken cutting.

Arrow Shot at Muslim Who Apostatizes Before Impact
Intra-Hanafi dispute on temporal scope of liability in killing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on liability when the target apostatizes after the act of shooting and explain why Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ.

Core Idea
Liability for shooting attaches at the moment of the act, not at impact.

When a shooter releases an arrow at a Muslim, but the target apostatizes before the arrow strikes, does the shooter still owe blood money? This passage from the Hidayah presents an intra-Hanafi dispute that hinges on whether liability is determined at the time of the act or at the time of the result. Imam Abu Hanifa argues the shooter's act is complete at shooting, fixing liability; the Sahibayn argue the target's apostasy destroys the protected value before impact, discharging the shooter. By studying this case, you will learn how the Hanafi school handles temporal gaps between act and result, and how shubah (doubt) affects qisas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The case: a Muslim shoots a Muslim; between the shot and the strike, the target apostatizes. Does the shooter pay diyah? Identify what each party considers the decisive moment.
2
State the Hanafi position
Imam Abu Hanifa holds diyah is obligatory. Understand his reasoning: the act of shooting is the source of liability, and at that moment the target had taqawwum (protected value).
taqawwum is the legal inviolability of a Muslim's life.
3
State the Sahibayn position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold no liability. Their reasoning: apostasy removes the target's taqawwum before the arrow hits, analogous to the victim forgiving the shooter after a wound but before death.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response
Abu Hanifa argues the analogy with forgiveness fails because forgiveness is a voluntary act of the victim, while apostasy is a change in status that cannot retroactively undo an already-completed act of shooting.
The shooter's act is complete; subsequent events cannot alter its legal consequences.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: under Abu Hanifa, the shooter pays diyah; under the Sahibayn, no compensation. Know which view is mufta bihi in later Hanafi practice.
The passage does not specify tarjih; later texts often follow Abu Hanifa.

ند دلک
ترجمہ اور فعل اور چند ہے ہاں قصاص شبکی وجہ سے ساتھ ہو گیا اور ذریت واجب ہوگی اور اگر اس کی طرف تیر پھینکا گیا اور وہ مرند ہے ہیں وہ مسلمان ہو گیا پھر اس کو تیر لگا تو اس پر کوئی بھی نہیں ہے ان تمامے قول میں اور ایسے بھی جب کہ اس نے حربی کو تیر مارا ہے
Chapter on Considering the State at the Time of Killing. Translation: This chapter is about considering the state at the time of killing. Explanation: States (ahwal) are from the category of attributes, and attributes precede essences. Therefore, after mentioning killing, the states are mentioned. A Muslim shot an arrow, and the one shot at apostatized after the arrow hit? Actually the text says: He shot a Muslim, then the target apostatized, then the arrow hit him; upon the shooter is diyah according to Abu Hanifa, and the two said nothing is upon him because by apostasy he abrogated the value of his own person, thus he became a discharger of the shooter from its obligation, just as if he discharged him after wounding before death. Explanation: ... (Then the dispute). And the proof of Abu Hanifa: He said: Liability is due by his act, which is the shooting, since there is no act from him after it. So the state at the time of shooting is considered, and the target at that time had value. For this reason the state at shooting is considered in matters of permissibility, so that it does not become forbidden by the shooter's apostasy after shooting; and likewise in matters of expiation, so that expiation is permissible after wounding before death. Explanation: ... And the act, even if intentional, qisas falls due to doubt and diyah becomes obligatory. And if he shot at him while he was an apostate, then he became Muslim, then the arrow hit him, there is nothing upon him in all their opinions. Similarly if he shot at a harbi then he became Muslim, because the shooting did not become an obligation of liability due to the lack of value of the locus at the time.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on liability when a shooter releases an arrow at a Muslim, but before the arrow strikes, the target apostatizes. The issue is whether liability attaches at the moment of shooting or at the moment of impact. Imam Abu Hanifa rules that diyah is obligatory because the act of shooting occurred when the target was still a Muslim (valuable person), and since the shooter did no further act, the state at shooting governs. Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad (Sahibayn) rule no liability because the target, by apostasy, destroyed his own legal inviolability (taqawwum) before impact, similar to the victim forgiving the assailant after a wound. The Sahibayn analogize this to a case where a victim forgives the assailant after injury but before death. Abu Hanifa counters that the shooter's act is complete at shooting; the target's later apostasy cannot undo liability. The text also clarifies that if the target was an apostate or harbi at the time of shooting and later becomes Muslim before impact, there is no liability by consensus (qawluhum jami'an). The mufta bihi position is not explicitly stated in this passage, but the structure of the Hidayah often presents the dispute with the first view being the relied-upon one; however, later Hanafi preference varies. The passage also touches on shubah (doubt) reducing intentional killing from qisas to diyah.

Key Terms
تقوم (taqawwum): Legal inviolability or protected value of a person's life, entitling them to blood money (diyah) or qisas.
شبهة (shubhah): Doubt or uncertainty that prevents qisas and converts it to diyah.
دية (diyah): Blood money paid for unintentional killing or when qisas is waived or cannot be applied.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling that diyah is due when a Muslim shoots a Muslim who then apostatizes before the arrow strikes?
A) The target was a Muslim at the moment of impact.
B) The shooter's act was complete at the time of shooting, and the target had taqawwum then.
C) Apostasy does not affect liability in any case.
D) The shooter must pay diyah for all intentional shootings.

Key Takeaway
Liability for an act of shooting is determined at the time of the act, not at the time of death, so apostasy after the act does not absolve the shooter.

Liability When Target's Status Changes: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability when target's status changes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between Imam Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions on liability when a target's legal status changes between shooting and impact, and apply the ruling to given scenarios involving apostasy or harbi status.

Core Idea
Liability is fixed at the moment of the act (Abu Hanifa) or at death (Sahibayn), depending on the target's taqawwum.

This passage examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the precise moment when liability for an intentional shooting is determined. The central variable is whether the target possesses legal viability (taqawwum) at the time of shooting or at the time of death. Understanding this disagreement clarifies the Hanafi methodology in handling cases where a person's legal status changes between the commission of an act and its consequences, and it reveals how different assumptions about the basis of liability lead to different rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the two Hanafi positions
Identify the views of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). Abu Hanifa considers the status at the time of shooting; the Sahibayn consider the status at the time of death.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not a cross-madhhab one.
2
Examine the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn argue that an apostate lacks taqawwum, so at death no liability remains. They analogize to a wounded person forgiving his assailant before death, which absolves liability.
Their qiyas is based on the idea that the status at death is analogous to forgiveness.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response
Abu Hanifa counters that the act of shooting is complete at the moment of release, and no further act from the shooter occurs. Therefore, the status at that moment should be considered. He supports this with examples from hunting and expiation.
The example: if a Muslim shoots game and then apostatizes, the game remains halal because the state at shooting is decisive.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
In cases where a Muslim shoots another Muslim who later apostatizes before death: Abu Hanifa holds liability (diyah wajib), Sahibayn hold no liability. In cases where the target is an apostate/harbi at shooting but becomes Muslim before impact, both agree no liability.
The agreed case shows that if the target lacks taqawwum at the time of shooting, the act never founds a basis for liability.

مضمان واجب نہ ہوگا۔ اور اس کی مثال بالکل ایسی ہے جیسے زخمی ہونے کے بعد مرنے سے پہلے زخم کرنے والے کو معاف کر دے تو وہ بری ہو جاتا ہے ایسا ہی یہاں بھی ہو گا۔ امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل وله ان الضمان يجب ابومنین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ مضمان رامی کے فعل سے واجب ہوتا ہے اور اس کا فعل رہی ہے اس لئے کہ رمی کے بعد رامی کی طرف سے کوئی عمل نہیں سے پاک راضی کی حالت کا اعتبار ہوگا اور مربی الیہ حالت رہی میں متقوم ہے اس وجہ سے شکار کی حالت کے حق میں ری کی حالت کا اعتبار ہوتا ہے۔ یہاں تک کہ شکار حرام نہ ہو کا رانی کی رہات کی وحدات حمل کے بعد اور ایسے ہی تعمیر کے حق میں یہاں تک کہ جو تانیہ جائز ہے موت سے پہلے زخم کے بعد۔ تشریح بیا امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل ہے جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ وقت رحمی کا اعتبار ہو گا کیونکہ رائی کی طرف سے رمی کے بعد کوئی فصل مصادر نہیں ہوا اور بوقت ریلی مربی الیہ مسلمان ہے بڑا اس وقت اس میں مقام ہے اور جب اس وقت اس میں اقوام ہے تو اس کا سامان واجب ہو گا اور بیت واجب ہوں ۔ جیسے اور کسی مسلمان نے کی شکار و تیر مارا اور تیر نکلنے سے ہے وہ شکاری مرتد ہو لیا تو شکار حلال ہوا میں کیونکہ بوقت رمی و یا مسلمان تھا اور حالت رہی ہیں معتبر ہے۔ لہذا آخر بعد رف قبل الاسعایت کنار و خط ادا کریں تو کیا رو ادا ہو جائے گا کیونکہ حالت ان کائی اعتبار ہے اس کے بعد جو مصنف نے فرمایات حتى جاز بعد الجرح قبل المرت مت ہے لیکن یہاں بے جوڑ ہے۔ یونکہ یہاں تو ری اور اصابت کے در میدان فرق بیان کرنا ہے اس لئے عبارت یوں ہونی چاہے تھے جاز التكفير بعد الرمي قبل الإصابة " ا پر تیر پھینکا اس حالت میں کہ مرکی الیہ مرتد یا حربی ہے پھر وہ مسلمان ہو گیا اور اسے تیر لگا تو یہ موجب ضمان نہیں والفعل وان كان عمدا فالقود سقط للشبة ووجب الدية ولو رمى اليه وهو مرتد فاسلم ثم وقع به السهم فلا عليه في قولهم جميعا و كذا اذا رمى حربيا فاسلم لأن الرمي ما انعقد بيا فاسلم لان الرمي ما انعقد موجبا للضمان لعدد تقوم المحل ترجمہ اور فعل اور چند ہے ہاں قصاص شبکی وجہ سے ساتھ ہو گیا اور ذریت واجب ہوگی اور اگر اس کی طرف تیر پھینکا گیا اور وہ مرند ہے ہیں وہ مسلمان ہو گیا پھر اس کو تیر لگا تو اس پر کوئی بھی نہیں ہے ان تمامے قول میں اور ایسے بھی جب کہ اس نے حربی کو تیر مارا ہے مارائیں وہ سوال یہ تیر پھینکنا تو عمد واقع ہوا ہے لہذا یہاں تو قصاص واجب ہونا چاہیئے تھا ؟ بات تو صحیح ہے مگر یہاں شبہ پیدا ہو گیا یعنی حالت اصابت کے اعتبار سے شبہ پیدا ہو گیا اس شبہ کی وجہ سے بجائے قصاص کے ت واجب ہوگی۔ اور اگر مری الیہ بوقت رمی مرتد تھا اور بوقت اصابت مسلمان ہے تو بالا تفاق نہ قصاص ہے اور نہ دیت ہے۔ اور اگر بوقت رمی وہ حربی کافر ہے اور بوقت اصابت وہ مسلمان ہے اب بھی کچھ ضمان واجب نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ بوقت رمی مرمی الیہ کے کی وجہ سے اس میں تقوم نہیں ہے۔ بلکہ اس میں تقوم بعد ری پیدا ہوا ہے اور جب رمی بوقت رمی کے لحاظ سے موجب عثمان نہیں بنی تو بعد میں تقوم آنے کی وجہ سے وہ موجب ضمان نہ ہو گی۔
The Sahibayn's proof is that the dead person is an apostate, so due to apostasy his taqawwum (legal viability) is eliminated. When taqawwum is eliminated, it is as if the apostate has absolved the shooter of liability before death. And when he absolves, no compensation is obligatory. Its example is exactly like if a wounded person forgives the one who wounded him before death, then he is absolved; similarly here. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof: 'And for him is that liability is established by his act, which is shooting, because there is no act from him after it, so the state at the time of shooting is considered, and the target at that time is viable.' Therefore, the state at shooting is considered regarding permissibility of game, even if the shooter becomes apostate after shooting; similarly regarding expiation, even if injury is allowed before death. Explanation: This is Imam Abu Hanifa's proof, whose summary is that the time of shooting is considered, because after shooting there is no new act from the shooter, and at the time of shooting the target is Muslim, so at that time it has value, so compensation is obligatory. For example, if a Muslim shoots game and after the arrow is released the shooter becomes apostate, the game is halal because at the time of shooting he was Muslim and the state at shooting is considered. So after [؟] before [؟] if he performs a line, it will be performed because the state at that time is considered. After that, the author said 'even if injury is allowed after the wound before death' but that is inconsistent here, because here the point is to distinguish between the state at shooting and at impact, so the expression should be 'allowed expiation after shooting before impact'. When he shoots at a target who is an apostate or harbi, then he becomes Muslim and is hit, this is not a cause of compensation. And the act, even if intentional, falls from qisas due to shubhah and diyah is wajib. And if he shoots at him while he is an apostate, then he becomes Muslim, then the arrow hits him, there is no liability upon him in all their sayings, similarly if he shoots a harbi and he becomes Muslim, because the shooting was not concluded as a cause for compensation due to the lack of taqawwum of the locus. Translation: 'And the act, even if intentional, qisas falls due to shubhah and diyah is wajib. And if he shoots at him and he is an apostate, then he becomes Muslim, then the arrow hits him, there is nothing upon him in all their sayings, and similarly when he shoots a harbi then he becomes Muslim.' Question: This arrow-throwing is intentional, so qisas should be obligatory here? The answer is correct, but here shubhah has occurred, that is, shubhah arises from the perspective of the state at impact, due to this shubhah, instead of qisas, diyah is wajib. And if the target at the time of shooting is an apostate and at the time of impact is Muslim, then by consensus there is neither qisas nor diyah. And if at the time of shooting he is a harbi infidel and at the time of impact he is Muslim, still no compensation is wajib, because at the time of shooting the target has no taqawwum due to [؟], rather taqawwum arose later, and when the shooting, from the perspective of the time of shooting, did not become a cause for compensation, then due to taqawwum appearing later, it does not become a cause for compensation.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding liability when a person shoots an arrow at a target whose legal status changes between the time of shooting and the time of impact. The Sahibayn argue that if the target is an apostate at the time of death, no compensation is due because an apostate's property and life lack legal viability (taqawwum). They analogize this to a wounded person forgiving his assailant before death, which absolves liability. Imam Abu Hanifa counters that liability is established by the act of shooting itself, and since no further act occurs from the shooter after that, the status at the time of shooting must be considered. If the target was a Muslim (or a legal person with taqawwum) at the moment of shooting, then liability is fixed regardless of later apostasy. He supports this with examples from hunting and expiation: if a Muslim shoots game and then becomes an apostate before hitting it, the game remains halal because the ruling depends on the state at shooting; similarly, if a Muslim shoots at game and then apostatizes, expiation is still required because the act was done as a Muslim. The text then clarifies a separate ruling: if the act was intentional, qisas is dropped due to shubhah (ambiguity) and diyah (blood money) becomes wajib. This shubhah arises because the target's status at the time of impact differs from that at the time of shooting. If the target was an apostate or harbi at the time of shooting but becomes Muslim before being hit, all scholars agree there is no liability because the act was not originally founded on a basis that would entail compensation (due to lack of taqawwum at the time of shooting). The passage emphasizes that the consideration of status at the time of shooting vs. impact is the key differentiating factor in determining liability in these cases.

Key Terms
تقوم (taqawwum): Legal viability or value of a person or property that makes compensation (daman) applicable. An apostate or harbi lacks taqawwum for purposes of civil liability.
ضمان (daman): Compensation or liability for harm caused, either through diyah (blood money) or payment of value.
شبه (shubhah): Ambiguity or doubt that prevents the application of a severe penalty (like qisas) and substitutes a lesser penalty (like diyah). Here, the change in the target's status creates shubhah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a Muslim shoots an arrow at a person who is a harbi (enemy combatant) and before the arrow hits, the harbi converts to Islam, what is the ruling according to all Hanafi scholars?
A) The shooter must pay diyah because the target became Muslim before death.
B) The shooter must pay full qisas because the killing was intentional.
C) There is no liability because the target lacked taqawwum at the time of shooting.
D) The shooter must pay both diyah and kaffarah.

Key Takeaway
Liability for shooting depends on the target's taqawwum at the moment of the act according to Abu Hanifa, or at death according to the Sahibayn, but both agree that if the target lacked taqawwum at the time of shooting, there is no liability.

Shooting a Slave Who Is Freed Before Impact: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability measure for shooting a slave freed before impact.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on compensation for shooting a slave who is freed before impact, identify the proof for each, and explain the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Emancipation severs the connection between act and death, so only the reduction in value is compensable, says Imam Muhammad.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute. A shooter fires at a slave, but before the arrow hits, the master frees him. The slave dies. The four Hanafi imams differ on how much the shooter must pay: full slave value, diyah, or the difference in value. Understanding their arguments requires grasping the concept of sirayah (continuity of injury).

Guided Path
1
State the facts of the case
Identify the timeline: Zayd's slave Bakr is shot by Khalid; before impact, Zayd frees Bakr; then the arrow hits and Bakr dies. The shooter is Khalid, the master Zayd, and the victim transitions from slave to free before death.
The change in status at the moment of death is critical for the dispute.
2
List the three Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (Shaykhayn): full value of the slave (as a slave) to the master. Zufar: diyah (blood money of a free person). Muhammad: the difference in value between the slave before shooting and after shooting (as a free man).
All agree compensation is wajib; only the measure differs.
3
Analyze each proof (talil)
Shaykhayn: liability attaches at the time of shooting when the victim is a slave with market value. Zufar: at death the victim is free, so diyah applies. Muhammad: emancipation cuts off sirayah (the continuity of injury from shooting to death), so only the direct harm caused by the shooting itself remains—i.e., the reduction in value from being a healthy slave to a wounded freedman.
Muhammad's key concept: al-‘itq qati‘ li al-siraayah.
4
Apply a concrete example to see the thamarah
Assume the slave was worth 1000 before shooting, and after shooting (as a free man) he is worth 200. Shaykhayn: 1000. Zufar: diyah (e.g., 5000). Muhammad: 200 (difference). This shows the practical divergence.
The difference is substantial: the shooter pays much less under Muhammad than under Zufar.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The text does not give a direct tarjih, but later Hanafi works (e.g., al-Hidayah commentary) generally adopt the Shaykhayn's view as the relied-upon position because the act is evaluated at the time of commission.
The mufta bihi is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کسی نے غلام کو تیر مارا تیر لگنے سے پہلے اس کے آقا نے اسے آزاد کر دیا پھر تیر لگا تو امی پر کیا چیز واجب ہے، اقوال فقہاء وان رمى عبدا فاعتقه مولاه ثم وقع السهم به فعليه قيمته للمولى عند ابي حنيفه وقال محمد عليه فضل غیر مرمی وقول ابى يوسف مع قول ابي حنيفة يخ زید کا غلام بکر ہے خالد نے بکر کو تیر مارا بوقت رمی وہ غلام ہے اور بوقت اصابت زید نے بکر کو آزاد کر دیا اور بکر اس تیر کی وجہ سے مر گیا تو اس میں ائمہ کا اختلاف ہے۔ حضرات شیخین نے فرمایا کہ صورت مذکورہ میں خالد، زید کیلئے بکر کی قیمت کا تاوان ادا کرے گا اور امام زفر نے فرمایا کہ خالد پر دیت ہوگی۔ اور امام محمد نے فرمایا کہ نہ دیت اور نہ قیمت بلکہ یہ دیکھا جائے کہ قبل رمی اس کی کیا قیمت تھی اور بوقت رمی کیا قیمت ہے ان دونوں درمیان جو تفاوت ہو گا وہی تفاوت خالد کے ذمہ واجب ہوگا۔ مثلاً قبل رمی اس کی قیمت ایک ہزار روپے ہے اور بعد ری اس کی قیمت ھ سوروپے ہے تو ان دونوں کے درمیان دو سوروپے کا تفاوت ہے یہی دو سو روپے واجب ہوں گے۔ اب امام محمد کی دلیل سنئے۔ امام محمد کی دلیل ان العتق قاطع للسراية واذا انقطعت بقى مجرد الرمي وهو جناية ينتقص بها قيمة المرمى اليه بالاضافة
Someone shot an arrow at a slave. Before the arrow hit, his master freed him. Then the arrow hit. So what is wajib upon him? The sayings of the jurists: 'And if he shoots a slave, then his master frees him, then the arrow hits him, then upon him is its value for the master according to Abu Hanifah. And Muhammad said: upon him is the difference between [the value of] the non-target and the target.' And the saying of Abu Yusuf is with the saying of Abu Hanifah. Explanation: Zayd's slave is Bakr. Khalid shot an arrow at Bakr. At the time of shooting, he is a slave. At the time of impact, Zayd freed Bakr, and Bakr died due to that arrow. So there is a disagreement among the Imams. The Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf) said that in the mentioned case, Khalid will pay compensation of Bakr's value to Zayd. Imam Zufar said that diyah is upon Khalid. Imam Muhammad said: neither diyah nor value, but rather it is seen what his value was before the shooting and what his value is at the time of shooting; the difference between the two is what is wajib upon Khalid. For example, before the shooting his value was 1000 rupees, and after shooting his value is 200 rupees, so the difference between them is 200 rupees; this 200 rupees will be wajib. Now hear Imam Muhammad's proof. Imam Muhammad's proof: 'Indeed, emancipation interrupts the [continuity of] injury, and when it is interrupted, nothing remains except the mere shooting, which is a crime by which the value of the target is diminished in addition.'
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a case where someone shoots an arrow at a slave, and before the arrow hits, the slave is freed by his master. The slave then dies from the arrow. The disagreement among the Hanafi imams is on the measure of compensation. According to the Shaykhayn (Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf), the shooter must pay the full value of the slave to the master, because the liability is based on the act of shooting when the victim was still a slave with a determinate value. Imam Zufar holds that diyah (blood money) is wajib, treating the victim as a free person at the time of death. Imam Muhammad takes a middle position: he argues that since emancipation severs the continuity (sirayah) of the injury from the initial act, the only remaining liability is for the diminution in value caused by the shooting. He compares the value of the slave before the shooting to his value after the shooting (after emancipation), and the difference (tafawut) is what is wajib. For example, if before shooting the slave was worth 1000 and after shooting (as a free man) he is worth 200, the shooter pays 200. Muhammad's proof is that emancipation cuts off the sirayah (the continuous process of injury), leaving only the initial act of shooting itself, which is a crime that reduces the victim's value. Therefore, the compensation is exactly the amount of that reduction. The text does not specify which position is mufta bihi, but this is a well-known dispute in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
سراية (sirayah): The continuity or extension of an injury from the initial act to the final result. Here, emancipation cuts off the sirayah, meaning the later death is not directly connected to the shooting because of the intervening act of freeing.
تفاوت (tafawut): The difference in value between the slave's worth before the shooting and his worth after the shooting (after emancipation) as a free man.
عتق (itq): Emancipation of a slave. Here, itq severs the sirayah and changes the legal status of the victim from property to a free person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad (al-Shaybani), if a slave is shot and then freed by his master before the arrow hits and dies, what compensation is owed?
A) The full value of the slave as a slave.
B) Diyah (blood money) for a free person.
C) The difference between the slave's value before shooting and after shooting (as a free man).
D) No compensation because the shooter did not intend to kill a free person.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi injury law, when a slave is freed before impact, the dominant (mufta bihi) view is that the shooter pays full slave value, because liability attaches at the time of the act.
